READING QUIZ#1 INFO STARTS HERE
Ordinances of Discovery - A law passed by the Spanish government that disallowed the
Spanish from military conquering and invading the Indigenous Peoples of America. This was to
incentivize the missionaries sent to the Americas to convert the Indigenous peoples of America
to Christianity rather than outright kill them because Christianity was a great way for the church
(and as a result the crown) to take control over a population.
Catholic Missions/Missionaries - People sent by the church to convert the Indigenous People of
America to Catholicism. Oftentimes they would have churches protected by a military fort, which
they called “Presidios”, which is where the Presidio (REMEMBER THE PLACIDIUM PRESIDIO)
gets its name from.
St. Augustine - Established in 1565. St Augustine was the first permanent Spanish settlement in
what is now the US. It originally served as a military outpost and headquarters for unsuccessful
military campaigns against the Indigenous Peoples of America?? (what does this mean dawg
💀) it means they wanted to kill natives.
Pueblo Revolt of 1680 - The Pueblo colony established by the Spanish forced the Pueblo
people in what is now Santa Fe to work long and hard with no benefit (Encomienda System),
while also forcing the Pueblo people to discontinue celebrating their culture. As a result, the
Pueblo people revolted and due to the pressure from this revolt, the Spanish were forced to
rework and almost entirely remove the Encomienda System, replacing it with a system in which
the Pueblo people were able to work a lot less for more benefit, and also freely celebrate their
cultures, as long as all newborn Pueblo people were baptized. This greatly increased the
strength of the relationship between the Spanish and Pueblo.
Spain’s Vast Empire - Spanish empire was massive, and more so economically than in any
other way. Due to their success in colonizing the South Americas, the Spanish forced the
Indigenous people there to dig for silver and gold in the Encomienda system, which allowed
them to produce literally 10x more free of the silver and gold than the entire world COMBINED.
That is nearly a 91% hold of the entire global silver and gold market at the time. Because of this,
Spain was able to conquer and take over so many portions of the world in the forms of colonies,
or if they were feeling generous trading outposts.

[PAGE 3]
Rigid Royal Control - Spain’s colonies in the Americas were controlled by the Spanish
government, and the Spanish government/crown made it a priority to make sure these colonies
were under strict control. (COME BACK TO LATER)
A Collision of Cultures - the contact between the Old World and American Indians in which both
groups absorbed aspects of the other, although different colonial powers interacted with Natives
differently, such as allowing them to continue speaking their own language or not, converting
them, etc.
Increasing Levels of Exchange - Refers to how heavily the Europeans and Indigenous peoples
affected each other, both positively and negatively. Europeans brought over livestock such as
horses(super important to the Indigenous people), while also bringing diseases that wiped out
entire populations of Indigenous people such as smallpox, influenza, measles, chicken pox,
mumps, and typhus. The Europeans also brought mass destruction to both the Indigenous
people and the surrounding environment. Indigenous people introduced new farming methods,
crops such as corn and potatoes, tobacco. In terms of the negatives, some Indigenous groups
were aggressive towards the Europeans, some even launched attacks on European
settlements(after being attacked first by the Europeans).
Demographic Catastrophe - Entire Indigenous populations were nearly or entirely wiped off the
face of the earth, with 95% of the Hispaniola population dying to disease or Christopher
Columbus and his weapons. These death counts are so high that it is believed that it far
outnumbers the Black Death’s death toll, which at the time had killed a third of the population of
Europe. This unintentional(but sometimes intentional) weapon caused genocide to nearly 90%
of all Indigenous populations that would maintain contact with the Europeans.
Deliberate Subjugation and Extermination - The Spanish used smallpox as a weapon,
devastating many indigenous peoples and weakening their armies.
New Crops and Agricultural Techniques - Because the crops that were brought to the Americas
would not prove to grow well or at all on American soil, the Indigenous people lended a hand by
introducing the Europeans to all sorts of different crops that we are very familiar with today, such
as potatoes, beans, peppers, tomatoes, sweet potatoes, pumpkins, squash and especially, corn,
which even today is in around what..75-80% of the world’s processed food(easy sugar). In
addition, the Indigenous people also taught the Americans unique farming techniques that
worked especially well in the Americas, such as girdling, where the trees are stripped of the
bark around the base, or setting fire to the roots so that the trees are devoid of nutrients.
A Complex Racial Hierarchy - Spanish Empire/Colonies implemented a racial hierarchy for the
Spanish colonies, with the White Spanish at the very top of the hierarchy, the mixed Mestizos,
who were quite common in the middle, and lastly of course the Indigenous peoples at the
bottom because uh. Europe + racism = <3 💪 (I love racism 😍😍😍😍(Jk don’t cancel me
guys 🙏))
Varied Labor System - European labor was mostly provided to by (basically enslaved)
Indigenous peoples, who were either full on slaves or were indentured servants who were…also
basically slaves because their servitudes were that long. However, this proved to be not enough
to fully break a profit or meet labor costs, so the Spanish started, you guessed it, importing
African Slaves (and indentured servants). (Who'da thunk it 😱)
Ghana and Mali - In Africa, there were 2 extremely large empires that had a large control and
reach over Western Africa, these 2 being Ghana and Mali. These 2 empires compromised their

[PAGE 4]
economies mostly of ivory, gold, and slaves who were mostly PoWs of the lands that either
empire conquered, criminals, or people who had big debts they couldn’t pay off. These 2
empires were extremely sophisticated and became the hubs for learning and development for
much of Africa, drawing in Europeans to explore the area and trade as well.
Benin, Congo, and Songhay - Benin, Congo, and Songhay were 3 smaller but somewhat
sizable countries in Africa that were South of the goliaths that were Ghana and Mali. These
kingdoms had economies of woven fabrics, ceramics, wooden and iron goods, and crops and
livestock. It is thanks to these economies that the African continent was able to function at such
a high pedigree at the time.
Matrilineal Societies - African states were mostly Matrilineal, which means that land ownership
and social aspects were mostly controlled by women. In marriage, it is expected that the man
moves into the woman’s family and home. Heritage was traced by mothers, and property was
inherited from mothers rather than fathers. Although the leaders of African states were all men,
the successors would always be the child of the former leader’s eldest daughter. In society,
women also played a significant role, with there often being split councils for women’s affairs,
Ain terms of gender equality, while European states were calling women witches for knowing the
cube root of 8. (T
f 8) (2) (Nah it can’t be two…)
Growth of the African Slave Trade - Due to the increasing need of African slaves to work
plantations in the Americas, the larger African empires saw this as an incredible financial
opportunity to export more African slaves to the Europeans, going to war with even more
kingdoms. At first, the Portuguese had control over the slave trade, then the Spanish, Dutch,
and lastly the English. These slaves were exported all over the Americas, but the initial most
popular destination was the Caribbean, because a majority of the people there had already
been wiped out by disease so the Europeans couldn’t forcefully enslave them, and also
because sugar, the primary export of that area, was super labor intensive.
John Cabot - An Italian explorer, first cunt to come to the New World after the Norse.
Commissioned by the British . John Cabot was mainly looking for a NorthWest passage through
the continent to get to what is now the Pacific ocean, but proved to be unsuccessful because it
didn’t exist.
The Enclosure Movement - A growing trend in England where bigtime landowners saw that wool
and sheep were a lot more profitable than normal crops due to higher wool demand (global mini
ice age from 1300-1850, we will see how this affects fur demand later), so they converted their
lands into pastures, and as a result kicked out the farmers who were there to farm crops,
sending thousands of farmers to homelessness, this was what was considered the “Enclosure
Movement”. who would have to beg for food and money, resorting to even robbing because of
how terrible their situation was. The English government did pass some laws to halt Enclosure,
but they proved to be unsuccessful, and with the majority of the English farms being used for
wool, the country found itself starving, with a population still heavily on the rise, giving even
more reason for the English to sponsor settlement in the Americas. Fields to Pastures
Chartered Companies - Group of investors form a company which is granted rights
by/incorporated into royal powers. Often the right for trade, exploration, and settlement (and
absolute control and rule over the area with no regard for the Indigenous populations that
already lived there :D)

[PAGE 5]
Mercantilism(wow never seen this one) - An economic policy that serves to maximize net profit
by cutting imports and maximizing exports and is state sponsored, as the idea of people
venturing out for goods and exports was not seen as a too popular option for governments to
sponsor in the past, but with the rise of the Portuguese empire it proved that mercantilism was
definitely the way to go . Supports national wealth as opposed to capitalism which is about
stuffing the pockets of a private individual or group.
Richard Hakluyt’s Argument for Colonies - Richard argued that colonies would create new
markets that would make England more rich. In addition, colonizing the new world would help
alleviate the overpopulation crisis undergoing in England.
Doctrine of Predestination - French Theologian John Calvin was an influential reformer after the
Protestant reformation led by Martin Luther. Him and his followers, the Calvinists(Boutta pull up
with my Yifeians) believed that a soul’s destiny was determined at birth for either salvation or
condemnation, and the only way to determine this was to see if you were useless to the society
around you or if you helped it, incentivising people to work hard to better the people around
them so that they could go, “aha! I am indeed destined for salvation!”
The English Reformation - A lot different from the Protestant reformation, the English
reformation was Crown vs Pope. King Henry VIII got pissy that he couldn’t divorce his wife
because he couldn’t get the son he wanted, so he broke England’s ties with the Catholic
church (And because he hated the pope(based)) , instating himself as the leader of Christianity in
England. Henry’s daughter queen Mary became queen of England and started a brutal
persecution of all those who did not convert to Catholicism, giving her the nickname of “Bloody
Mary” (Bloody Mary my beloved 😍) . England was basically in an insane religious turmoil.
Puritan Separatists - Protestants that became unhappy with the church due to the church’s lack
of reformation became the Puritans, because they wanted to “Purify” the church. Separatists
refers to the radical ideas that the Puritans had and how they wanted to become independent of
the Church of England, making them “Puritan Separatists”. This dissatisfaction with the Church
of England and the oppression and discrimination they received from the government and crown
in England gave them good reason to move to the American colonies, making them a large
portion of the population in America, and heavily impacting American colonies, especially those
more North such as Massachusetts.
Puritan Discontent - Queen Elizabeth’s successor James I antagonized Puritans, resorting to
random taxation(what a classic!), granting Catholics charters and favors. Because of this, many
Puritans saw the new land in the Americas as possibly a place that could prove to be of great
benefit to them as a recluse from England.
Subjugation of Ireland - Basically England says “It’s gamer time” and invades Ireland, calling
them “savages” and “uncivilized” (wow I wonder if they will say this to any other peoples and
ethnicities later on?!). This invasion brought fame to some people who governed over the newly
attained English colonies in Ireland, including a certain Sir Humphrey Gilbert and Sir Walter
Raleigh. Their violent tactics in suppressing the Irish populations would prove to inspire later
English tactics in the Americas.
The Plantation Model - The English were very unwilling to integrate into Indigenous society and
live alongside them unlike the Spanish and French, and as a result started their Plantations,
which were plots of land that were fenced off from Indigenous people(literally or figuratively) and
would serve as little plots of English society, which was inspired by the exact same model that

[PAGE 6]
was used in Ireland. Now lemme tell you why this is not the best idea: Ireland geographically is
a lot more familiar to the British, so growing crops and stuff is somewhat easy and doable, and
m.so is traversing the land and building on it. The American colonies are 4.4k nautical miles
away from England, and DO NOT have similar geography to England. You can see where this is
going can you…
Coureurs De Bois - Term used for French fur hunters/trappers. These guys didn’t actually
physically hunt the animals used for the furs and pelts they sold to Europe most of the time
actually, but acted as a middleman between the Indigenous peoples and Europe, making big
bank from the process, as not only could they charge a high price to the Europeans while
buying low from the Indigenous peoples, but the global demand for furs and pelts at the time
was incredibly high as as said before, the world is in a mini ice age from 1300-1850.
Henry Hudson - Pictures of this guy are not actually him! There are no real paintings of him that
actually (officially) exist today. Henry Hudson was an English explorer employed by the Dutch to
sail up the river which is now named the Hudson River because uh you know, Europeans. He
would eventually find the area that is now New York, and establish a Dutch colony in the
Americas.
New Amsterdam - New Netherlands became the new Dutch colony up North of the American
coast, with New Amsterdam as its largest and central town, which will see staggering growth in
its lifespan. The colony was able to survive thanks to the implementation of the patroons
system, where landlords were granted ownership of lands in the Dutch colony in exchange for
the promise they would bring more people to the Dutch colony.
Spanish Armada - The Spanish Armada was basically the Spanish military fleet. This fleet was
extremely feared as the sheer size of it made it incredibly strong and dangerous, and at the
time, the Spanish and English were at conflict/butting heads with each other, and to spice things
up (idfk im tired), the Spanish decided to bring the Armada across the English channel to
directly attack the English, which failed to a much smaller English fleet. This greatly weakened
the Spanish economy and global presence because the colonies that were held by the Spanish
were government sponsored and controlled, and the military going to shit kinda destroys your
economy and that of your state owned colonies 😬. On the flip side, this gave the English the
100% go ahead to start developing their own colonies.
Gilbert’s Expedition to Newfoundland - Sir Humpherey Gilbert got permission from Queen
Elizabeth for 6 years to freely travel and take over any un-European-claimed(?) in the Americas.
Gil was unsuccessful in finding a spot, his ship sinking and lost at sea, but his buddy Walter
Raleigh kept on pushing with his ship, eventually finding himself also 6 years to freely travel
through the Americas to find a spot to build a possible settlement, and that he did, sending a
group of men to the Americas, where they found out from captures Indigenous peoples that an
island named Roanoke could possible serve as a spot for a colony.
The First Roanoke Colony - Richard Grenville left settlers on the island of Roanoke which had
earlier been found by Sir Walter Raleigh’s men, and promptly destroyed the Indigenous people’s
village over a minor theft, and went back to England for some unspecified reason, leaving
behind a couple colonists (thanks textbook). Sir Francis Drake then arrives in Roanoke with
supplies meant for the remaining colonists, taking them home cuz they were starving and dying.

[PAGE 7]
Attempt 2! Raleigh sends more people this time, led by John White. John White after dropping
the folks off and helping out a little goes back to England, where his return to the colony is
delayed due to a Spanish English conflict, and when he goes back he finds the colony
completely deserted, with nothing but “Croatoan” written on a post, which in the textbook has no
meaning but a Lemmino video has some PRETTY damn good evidence that all the Roanoke
settlers moved to a nearby island, where they integrated into the Indigenous people’s society
there (as is evident from some of the Indigenous people having blue eyes or lighter skin).
New Colonial Charters - This charter divided the American colonies in 2, the London group got
the charter to the south, and the Plymouth colony got a charter for the north. May or may not
have contributed to the very very different culture and political view differences in the North and
South (wow is that more Civil War foreshadowing??)
Early Problems - James I had 144 men board 3 ships to the American coast and into what is
now Chesapeake and up the James river that they named, establishing Jamestown. The area
sucked though, swamps, thick woods, and disease such as malaria ravaged the initial settlers.
The settlers brought no women with them, and everybody was too focused on getting gold for
some odd reason. Only thanks to the help of the nearby Indigenous people that they were able
to survive, who taught them to grow crops and how to cultivate the land.
The Starving Time - 600 more settlers came to Jamestown, including women and children. This
proved to be not the best idea though, since in 1609 the winter was uh. Out for blood. Due to a
severe lack of planning, any agriculture whatsoever, and malaria, everyone was starving and
dying and also pretty miserable because of starving and everyone dying. The Jamestown
settlers ate literally anything, dogs cats, rats, snakes, toadstools, the soles of their shoes, tree
bark, and even dead human bodies. The winter was so harsh, that of the 6-700 people there,
only 60 lived, however, thanks to some awesome stuff we call lies and deceit, more migrants
came, expecting to find the Jamestown colony in full swing, but instead finding nothing but 60
starving and probably mentally scarred settlers.
De La Warr’s Harsh Discipline - Tobacco was found out by the English, and as a result the
English quickly started to grow tobacco to fund their colony, which they became crazy rich off of.
However, the leader of the plantations, De La Warr proved to be a very unpopular and strict
leader, often flogging, hanging, or “breaking on the wheel”(basically you are tied to a large
wooden wheel, and uh. I don’t need to finish that but you get the idea) anybody who wouldn’t
work when they were supposed to.
Wasn’t very popular, so his successor tried using positive reinforcement. Work and you get
land!!! Yay!!!!
Emergence of the Tobacco Economy - John Rolfe decided to try to crossbreed a South
American and North American tobacco (Illegal! ) and with the help of the Indigenous people,
succeeded, making an incredibly efficient and addictive tobacco plant that would make him
RACKS in the global economy at the time, and anyone that had the bravery to travel over the
the Americas to grow tobacco. This would cause the English to move deep into the Indigenous
land in prospect for more land to grow more tobacco, breaking the once good relationship
between the Indigenous people and the English.
The Headright System - Headrights were 50 acre guarantees given to anybody that would settle
the American colonies by the Crown of England. This was to incentivize the people of England
to move to the Americas so that not only England would get rid of a growing problem of the

[PAGE 8]
population not being able to feed itself, but also so that they could rake in some of the tobacco
cash. Europeans loved it! Indigenous people? Not so much…
Suppression of the Powhatan Indians - The Powhatan Confederacy, a collection of groups of
Indigenous peoples that were brought together by the chief Powhatan lived in the
Virginia(Jamestown) area obviously long before the English arrived. Sir Thomas Dale led
extremely violent and successful attacks on the Powhatan Confederacy members, capturing
Pocahontas(not actually her real name btw), the daughter of Powhatan, and got married to John
Rolfe, who brought her back to England where everybody was shocked that Powhatan, the
daughter of the chief of an entire confederacy decided to marry a lower class person like John
Rolfe, which is kinda ironic considering you’d think people would be more shocked that John
Rolfe would marry and bring back someone the English considered “savages”. Pocahontas was
actually a great asset for both the Powhatan and the English, and helped soften relations
between the English and Powhatan by being a translator.
Demise of the Virginia Company - The Virginia Company, the company that had been
sponsoring so many of the Jamestown pursuits had actually failed to satisfy the crown and the
conflict with Indigenous peoples there turned out to be pretty unpopular back at home, so
James I revoked their charter, with the Jamestown colony becoming under control of the English
crown up until 1776.
Indian Agricultural Techniques - The Indigenous peoples of America native to the East Coast
had many agricultural techniques that hard carried the Europeans settlers, such as girdling
trees, which removes the bark from the bottom of the tree or by setting fire to the roots, which is
actually quite smart now that I think of it. This is like the 5th time I’ve said this but as always, the
Indigenous people taught the Europeans how to grow crops like corn, potatoes, and tomatoes,
which were extremely important, especially corn, yada yada.
Proprietary Rule - The Maryland colonies had started due to a father’s wish for a land he never
visited to become a recluse for English catholics, his name was George Calvert, and his
successors would do good on his wish. Cecilius Calvert, his son, received a charter from the
king that allowed him control over Pennsylvania, Delaware, and Virginia, and Calvert had
“Proprietary Rule” over the land, in which he was the “true and absolute” lord (so basically 100%
control) of the land, and all he had to do was pay an annual fee to the crown.
Religious Toleration - Because of the number of Protestants leaving England, Maryland’s
population actually became majority Protestant despite it being meant more for Catholics, so
Cecilius Calvert, the leader at the time of Maryland established a Religious Toleration act,
allowing for any and all Christians to celebrate the sects they chose to.
Virginia’s Westward Expansion - Virginia continued to move Westward into Indigenous
controlled areas, and border conflicts were very common (wow who would’ve thought it the
Indigenous people don’t like their land being taken). The Indigenous people however were no
match for the English brutality and guns, so Virginia’s Westward expansion proved to be pretty
smooth (and by that I mean incredibly bloody for the Indigenous) and quick.
Berkeley’s Autocratic Rule - Sir William Berkeley, the governor of Virginia, becomes an Autocrat,
which means that he is the person in power, and people who are loyal to him will stay in their
positions of government. This leads to people being severely underrepresented, especially folks
who live in the backcountry.

[PAGE 9]
Backcountry Grievances - So this guy named Nathaniel Bacon is part of Berkeley’s council, but
he’s against Berkeley for numerous reasons. First of which being that Bacon and his posse Not
just his posse, but most of the people in less developed areas of Virginia. are constantly getting
attacked by indigenous people, which leads to Bacon disagreeing with Berkely’s more passive
rules about the indigenous people. Secondly, Bacon is upset that he’s not part of the inner
government, and that he’s not making any money from the fur trade.
Significance of Bacon’s Rebellion - Nathaniel Bacon, a wealthy aristocrat and Cambridge Uni
graduate had arrived in Virginia when it was under the control of William Berkley. Bacon was
ambitious and hated Berkley because Berkley didn’t let him have a portion of the fur trade that
was shared between the English and Indigenous people, in addition, Bacon was also not
allowed into the inner governing circle of Virginia, making him PISSED. After a Doeg
(Indigenous people) attack on an English plantation, Bacon led his own counter attack on the
Doeg, where he proclaimed himself a rebel, as he did the counter attack on his own and without
permission. Now, Bacon had a reason to rebel against Berkeley. Him and others that were
unhappy with the landlords of Virginia, mainly lower class workers, indentured servants, or
slaves, not exclusive to race so both English and African peoples joined in, led a revolt on
Berkeley, which the outcome is just completely left out in the textbook :) thanks. THE
SIGNIFICANCE: It proved that no matter what race, low income people were low income
people, and when the system they are in clearly oppresses them and forces them into a certain
financial class, they will unite. This would lead to slavery being tied to race in fear of the African
and English low incomes uniting again to bring the high income aristocrats and landlords out of
power. The thought was, by separating the English from African slaves, the English would not
see the Africans at the same level, and it worked I guess. cooked system, but we all knew that
already
Religious Repression - Puritan Separatists, who wanted to cut ties with the Church of English
and wanted to be able to celebrate their own Christian theology were continuously persecuted
and the American colonies did not seem like a bad idea.
The Mayflower Compact - Some Puritans in England, unhappy with the treatment they had at
home decided to move over to the American colonies, traveling aboard the Mayflower, they left
Plymouth(ENGLISH PORT, DON’T MIX IT UP), and although the destination was supposed to
be the Hudson River mouth, they found themselves in what is now Cape Cod, arriving in an
area named Plymouth which had been named by Captain John Smith some years before. That
was confusing, let me sum it up: Pilgrims leave Plymouth, England, and arrive in Plymouth
Americas, which got its name PRIOR to the Pilgrims arrival by John Smith who had already
been there before. The Mayflower Compact just basically said that the Pilgrims would establish
a civil government and would be loyal to the Crown of England.
Relations with the Indians - Ah but there’s more this time in comparison to the 20 other times
I’ve talked about Indigenous English relationships. Some Indigenous people that were captured
by or decided to accompany the English learned and picked up English proved to be of great
help by translating for the English settlers, and sHoWiNg HOw tO pLaNT cORn, PotAtoeS, etc.
Some of these awesome dudes included Squanto and Samoset. Squanto was a Pawtuxet, Ms.
Bellas told us this wild story of how Squanto got kidnapped to England, learned English, and
somehow made it back to the same area but his tribe died of disease so he was kinda just
there. Samoset was uh hurr durr look guys I’m APUSH textbook and REFUSE to admit my

[PAGE 10]
mistakes or lack of specific information so instead I will leave this information up to mystery and
speculation jaja :DDDDD good luck on a bs test you gotta pay for!!!! Back to what I was saying,
thanks to these guys, the Wampanoags became allies with the English, and baddabing
baddaboom first thanksgiving in 1621 yahoo!
Also more specifically, the Pilgrims had a relatively positive relationship with the Wampanoag
tribe, especially given Squanto’s abilities as an interpreter.
William Bradford - Plymouth chose William Bradford as the leader of the Plymouth colony,
receiving a charter from the Council of New England so that he could freely do whatever he
pleased in the Plymouth area.
Massachusetts Bay Company - Back in England, many Puritans had had enough(and I’ve had
enough with telling you that Puritans are pissed) thanks to Charles I, who was the successor of
James I, who really was not a fan of anybody that wasn’t a Roman Catholic, and would imprison
Puritans just for their beliefs, although not as much as some other English rulers, so a large
group of them moved over to what is currently Massachusetts to start a new colony. These guys
were mostly merchants, as were most Puritans, and as a result started the Massachusetts Bay
Company. Now how did they even get permission to go to Massachusetts? Well, they simply got
a charter from King Charles I, who was completely unaware that these guys were Puritans. The
company made a large portion of revenue from fishing and the other early American colony
economy stuff like tobacco, corn, trading with the Indigenous people, and so forth.
John Winthrop - John Winthrop was selected as the governor of the company and as a result
the Massachusetts area, and under his rule Massachusetts started to grow like crazy, although
most likely due to the insane work ethic that a majority of the Massachusetts Bay Company had,
with Boston being quite a decently sized town, and other towns popping up like crazy, such as
Charleston, Newtown(later Cambridge), Roxbury, Dorchester, Watertown, Ipswitch, Concord,

A Theocratic Society - The Massachusetts colony was considered a “theocratic society”
because oftentimes the government would come to the aid of the church in the form of helping
out ministers, who backed the church but had no official political power. This made it so that
anybody who opposed the church or the Ministers would be punished or treated the same as
breaking a law, so it was kind of easy to say that Massachusetts was simply run by the churches
that it supported.
Growing Religious Dissent - More non-Puritans came to Massachusetts, and these people could
not vote because they were not considered Puritan “saints”. Most of these folks left for New
England or Connecticut. One important person in particular that left Massachusetts was Thomas
Hooker, who was a minister of Newtown (later named Cambridge) due to the ways he defied the
Massachusetts government, and left for Connecticut, establishing Hartford, and those Hartford
guys later established two other towns.
Roger Williams - Roger Williams was basically this one guy who nobody agreed with but
everybody loved. It’s like that one uncle with different political views but always treats his kids,
nephews and nieces with good food and gifts at reunions. Roger Williams was a Separatist, and
argued that the Massachusetts church should cut ALL TIES with the Church of England, which
would definitely put a big target on Massachusetts’ back. He also called for the Church to
completely separate from the state and government, which also didn’t fare too nicely with
everybody in Massachusetts. This did not mean he didn’t have followers however, and promptly
moved to Rhode Island after getting a charter from English Parliament, even getting permission
on the charter to “liberty in religious concernments”.
Anne Hutchinson - Anne Hutchinson was a woman that was highly educated in the study of
Theology, and had some at the time radical thoughts about the church. She antagonized many
of the Massachusetts members for not being part of the “elect”, basically saying “you ain’t fit fo
yo role foo”. After displaying pretty reasonable evidence and gaining a very large following of
both men and women, the Massachusetts government decided to intervene, putting her on trial

[PAGE 12]
for heresy, where she was convicted for being a “woman not fit for our society”. She also moved
to Rhode Island and then New Netherland. Although her life would be ended short by an
Indigenous attack, her legacy would live on as seen in the next term.
New Hampshire and Maine - John Mason and Fernandnino Gorges would get the rights to the
New Hamsphire land and would split it up while Mason is known for attracting the earlier settler
there.Some time after that, John Wheelwright, a disciple and follower of Anne Hutchinson led
some other like minded people to Exeter, which is located in New Hampshire, with more people
later following, with New Hampshire breaking off from Massachusetts, and Maine later in 1820.
Importance of Indian Assistance - The Indigenous people helped with agriculture, teaching the
English how to farm the crops native to the Americas, such as corn, potatoes, tomatoes, beans,
sweet potatoes, squash and pumpkins, with corn bEiNG tHe mOsT ImPoRTaNT. They also
learned agricultural techniques such as annual burning so the soil received fresh fertilizer. The
Indigenous people were also the primary trade partner for the Europeans, trading iron pots,
blankets, metal-tipped arrows in contrast to the flint or obsidian arrows used by the Indigenous
people, guns, and unfortunately alcohol to the Indigenous people in exchange for…THE

Capture of New Amsterdam pg51 - Dutch and English were under heavy competition with each
other in the colonies, competing over trade territory and commercial gain. English in particular
saw the Dutch as a major problem, and would come to resent the Dutch colonies for being a
wedge between the North and South English colonies. Because of this, the English sailed into
the New Amsterdam port and took over the New Amsterdam area under the Articles of
Capitulation.
Establishment of New Jersey pg52 - New Jersey was founded when one of James II political
allies was given a charter for the land from James II. This charter would prove to be somewhat
unsuccessful in getting profit for the colony, and eventually New Jersey became a royal colony
under James's rule. New Jersey, similar to New York, was incredibly diverse. However, New
Jersey’s industry basically stayed as just farming and didn’t really move forward anywhere.
The Society of Friends pg52 - The Society of Friends was the formal name for the Quakers who
would eventually inhabit Pennsylvania. They were also Protestants who were prosecuted in
England looking for a new place to live without fear of a crown cracking down on them. Quakers
rejected the ideas that people had predestined fates and instead chose to believe that there was
good in everybody and that everybody deserves a chance. Another cool thing about these folks
was that they really believed in the idea that women in the church could have the same
positions and power as men. In Quaker churches, the common tradition was for people to sit in
a circle and shout whatever came to their mind that could be interpreted as the word of God. A
bit unorthodox but a nice fresh change and also definitely a more liberal and accepting style.

The English Caribbean pg54 - Before 1600, the main destination for the English in America was
actually the Caribbean, where they would eventually move out because the Spanish colonies
surrounded the English ones, resulting in the English colonists opting instead to move to the
mainland.
Imperial Conflict pg54 - Before the Europeans arrived in the Caribbean, there were already a

population wiped out by European diseases. Eventually, more European empires came to the
Caribbean, including the Dutch and English, who served as major competition for the Caribbean
area for the Spanish.

James Oglethorpe’s Vision pg57 - General James Oglethorpe was a member of Parliament and

Georgia’s Military Rationale pg59 - To make it easier for Georgia to defend itself, the military
consolidated more land in order to have a more centralized and compact defense. The Georgia
colony made sure to exclude African peoples entirely, free or slaves from its defense so that
they not only did not have to accommodate and be responsible for them, but also so that they

freeing slaves in exchange for military service.
Transformation of Georgia pg60 - James quickly saw Georgia’s situation as a colony
deteriorate, and it was clear to him that his way of ruling things nearly as a dictatorship was not
going to work. His people complained that the lack of slavery made labor extremely expensive
and hard, and the overall growth of the colony was basically stagnant. James removed the ban
on slavery later, and eventually Georgia became a Royal Colony.
Conflict of Accomodation pg60 - The “Middle Grounds” were English colonies that although
living next to the Indigenous peoples and not directly invading their land, they still lived quite

[PAGE 18]
apart and seemed to be near conflict with them at all times. Basically they weren’t constantly
invading Indigenous land, but they were not living amongst them on super friendly terms like the
French.
Mutually Beneficial Relations pg60 - The French, who were looking to the colonies more as a
financial benefactor made sure to maintain good relationships with the Indigenous people in the
areas they created colonies. They respected Indigenous customs and traditions and recognized
the tribal chiefs amongst the Indigenous people, allowing for the French to have the best initial
relationship with the Indigenous people. The French would also often marry into Indigenous
families in order to maintain good relationships with the Indigenous people.
The Shifting Balance pg60 - As America gained its independence from the English and saw the
need to expand Westward, the English colonies destroyed many of the good relationships they
had with the Indigenous people, causing the Middle Grounds to all but never exist.
Mercantilism pg60 - bruh.
The Navigation Acts pg61 - As the English government found that the American colonies were
making a majority of their profits by selling to the Spanish and Dutch, Parliament made sure to
do something about this so that the English economy would be able to better expedite the
colonies’ earnings. They implemented the Navigation Acts, a collection of 3 laws that would
attempt to prevent the American Colonies from trading to non-English countries:
1. The first one implemented would prevent the English from trading with any ships outside
of English ships, preventing any exports from major colonies from leaving the English
territories
2. The second one saw that all the products exported out from English colonies had to
pass through England, allowing them to tax all the ships that would come through the
English channel.
3. The third law just went ahead and told customs that they had to crack down on anybody
who wouldn’t follow the navigation acts and told people to follow them and all of that.

Exceptional Longevity in New England pg68 - As diseases and the risks for people in the New

residents, with one generation even averaging out to 70 for both men and women.

also had a large portion of their population be female, so overtime, the 3:1 or 4:1 ratio of men to
women slowly became 3:2 or 1:1.

More Diverse Economy in North pg78 - In the North, agriculture was THE(no sarcasm here)
most important part of the economy. But unlike in the South, the colonies in the north were less
dominated by farming. The northern economy was more diverse than the economy in the South
because conditions for farming were less favorable there. Northern New England in particular
had a very hard time farming, the cold weather, hard rocky soil made it almost impossible to set
up a large-scale farming system that the southern colonies were creating. Southern New
England and the middle colonies had an easier time farming. New York, Pennsylvania, and the
Connecticut River valley were the chief suppliers of wheat to a lot of New England and even
parts of the south. Yet even in those places, a substantial commercial economy emerged
alongside the agricultural one. Nearly every colonist engaged in a certain amount of industry.
Sometimes these industries provided families with extra goods they could trade or sell.
Craftsmen and artisans established themselves in colonial towns as cobblers, blacksmiths,
riflemakers, and more.
Saugus Ironworks pg79 - The Saugus metalworks facility was the first effort that the colonists
made to establish a significant metals industry in the colonies. After iron ore deposits were
discovered in the 1640s, the colonists tried to transfer Iron technology already present in
England to the colonies. The Saugus works used water power to drive a bellows, which
controlled the heat in a charcoal furnace that melted the ore. As said ore melted, it trickled down
into molds to then be inscribed with dark magic to make tools. While it was a technological
success, it was a financial failure, and the Saugus ironworks closed its doors in 1668. (Book
goes on to talk about metalworks)
Extractive Industries pg80 - More important than manufacturing were industries that exploited
the natural resources of the continent. By the mid-seventeenth century, the flourishing fur trade
of earlier years was in decline. Replacing it were lumbering, mining, and fishing. These
industries provided commodities that could be exported to England in exchange for
manufactured goods. They also helped produce the most distinctive feature of the northern
economy: A COMMERCIAL CLASS(allegedly thriving)

[PAGE 22]
Myth of Self-Sufficiency pg80 - Unlike popular belief, very few colonists were self-sufficient. The
colonists purchased whatever they needed or could afford. Most farmers who grew grain took it
to centralized facilities for processing
Shortage of currency pg80 - Due to the lack of safety and stability in the colonies, there is no
real currency that remains stable or usable. A lack of coinage also leads to people using
bartering or trading goods as currency.
Triangular Trade pg80 - In simple terms, this describes the trade of Sugar, Rum, and Slaves
among England, the Americas and Indies, and Africa. However this is heavily simplified, and
there was a lot more trade of a lot more goods, such as rice, molasses, guns, manufactured
goods, tobacco, and other crops and resources. (more info about it in the packet we got on
1/9/23)
Emerging Merchant Class pg81 - The Merchant class was formed by a group of adventurous
entrepreneurs. For reasons unbeknownst to us(cuz the book sucks), they formed the merchant
class. This class enjoyed protection from foreign competition within the English colonies, as the
British Navigation Acts had excluded all non-British ships from the colonies carrying trade. This
merchant class had access to a market in England that needed American products like furs,
timber, and ships. But this market did not satisfy all their commercial needs. Many of the colonial
products could be easily produced by England themselves, and they needed another market
outside the British Empire. So they did a funny and ignored laws restricting colonial trade to
England; many merchants developed markets in the French, Spanish, and Dutch West Indies,
where prices were often higher than in the British colonies. The profits from this illegal activity
enabled the colonies to import the manufactured goods they needed from Europe.
Growing Consumerism pg 82 - In the 18th century, consumerism saw its way into American
society, especially in cities. This was widely due to the financial stability most city families found
themselves in in colonial America thanks to growing industries such as ironworks or tobacco
plantations. Consumerism also began to grow in the American colonies thanks to the early
stages of the industrial revolution. The industrial revolution contributed to American
consumerism because European nations started to produce high quality goods and products
that could be shipped to America, and thanks to the high income of most Americans in cities,
many Americans saw a good amount of luxury European goods displayed or in use in their
homes.
Social Consequences pg 82 - Thanks to the growth of consumerism, Americans saw a reason
to not only purchase high quality goods, but act like they were of a high class as well, leading to
a trend in which high class Americans saw to get educated and to act refined or
“gentlemanlike/ladylike”, buying English magazines on how to act like the richest of the rich
back in England, commissioning portraits of themselves and their families, got fancy furniture,
and put a fair amount of effort into appearing fashionable for the time.
Social Mobility pg 82 - Social mobility refers to the way that people move up and down in the
social hierarchy that is present in society back then and today. For example, England was
unable to have a high social mobility rate, since land was super scarce for how insanely high the
population was at the time. Land ownership was almost nonexistent and the people who did
own land would often be at the top of the financial/social ladder for ages until they for some
reason got rid of their land, simply because land means power. Whereas in the colonies, it was
the exact opposite. Land was abundant and people were scarce, allowing for better social

[PAGE 23]
mobility amongst the people, as people could easily obtain land and with enough smart choice
and hard work(slave labor) could easily move up the social ladder. This shows how social
mobility was a lot more present in colonial America in comparison to the rest of the world.
Vagaries of the Plantation Economy pg 83 - Real quick; “vagaries” refers to a change in
behavior for those who don’t know. Basically all this keyword is saying is that tobacco
plantations early on would always make either a lot of money or very little. This is because most
plantations were 100% reliant on the price of one crop, mainly tobacco. This weeded out the
bad plantation owners from the good ones, since the planters who were better would be able to
weather the storm and not have to worry about going bankrupt during bad times.
Stratified Southern Society pg 83 - Stratified in a social context refers to a hierarchical format,
where those who are the wealthiest are at the top with the most power, vice versa. In the South,
with how important plantations were, plantation owners with the most land could easily control
the people who worked on their plantations, including the smaller farmers who would work on
the plantations but without the smaller farmers, the plantation owners couldn’t turn a profit. Still,
the plantation owners were the richest out of the American South, and dominated the market
despite the small farmers and slaves making up the majority of the population.
Slave Culture pg 84 - ooh this was one of the more fun parts of the book to read since I never
knew about anything related to this, and it’s honestly just interesting. American slaves, due to
them being in close proximity with each other(in terms of those who worked the same
plantation) formed very close bonds with each other and developed an intricate culture unique
to slaves. This included things such as Gullah, a language that was a blend of English and
African languages as to not allow the slave owners to get in on all of their conversations, and a
distinct religion which combined Christianity and African religions. The textbook however, gives
0 examples which is just…wonderful. African slaves would also often start their own gardens
and farms next to the quarters where they would live to grow their own food, with the seeds
mostly being provided by the plantation owners or most likely taken/given from other farms.
Slaves often had such close bonds that even new slaves who were not in the families of the
slaves of the existing plantation would be welcomed as relatives and treated like family.
Stono Rebellion pg 85 - In 1739, 100 or so African slaves rose up and killed several white
people and attempted an escape to Florida, but eventually had some White people quickly
capturing them. This is important because it shows just how futile it was for African slaves to
attempt to resist the White plantation owners. Slaves would often escape, but upon capture
(which was common) they’d be sent back to their plantation where they would be SUPER
heavily punished. American slavery in the south was by far the worst that slavery had been at
any point in history, despite the history of slavery stretching all the way back to the first human
civilization. This established some new slave codes for 1740 (1705 was the first slave code
btw). (BTW this was the beating drums rebellion that we learned in class)
Patterns of Settlement pg 85 - Puritan settlements began drawing up “covenants”, a system in
which all of its members in the settlement were connected in “unity and harmony” and I bet like
God’s piss or something idfk. In this settlement, colonists would lay out a village with houses
around a central pasture, otherwise known as the “common”, and divide the fields and
woodlands of the town amongst its residents. This helped reinforce a strong sense of
community in Puritan societies in the Americas.

[PAGE 24]
Puritan Democracy pg 86 - Puritan societies ran on yearly town meetings in which a group of
adult males named the “selectmen” would discuss issues and problems regarding the
settlement. This was also decided based off of church membership status, those who were part
of the church would be among the elect, or “visible saints”, while those who were not would
need conversion, and were the “halfway covenant”
Population Pressure pg 86 - Because of the population growth of the Puritan settlements, more
and more people began to search for new land to farm and own. This caused people to move
farther and farther away from the community centers that Puritans had set up generations
before. This would cause people far away from their own community centers to feel the need to
build their own facilities such as churches, starting their own communities. This is kind of like
cell growth.
Generational Independence pg 86 - Despite Puritan societies being majority Patriarchy, in
practice this was just in name. Farmland was hard to work, and everyone had to chip in to help
feed the large families, wives and daughters too. Even though males still had a majority of the
control in a family, the voices of the females of the family were always heard out of necessity for
their work, and also obviously because they’re families and respect each other.
Salem Witch Trials pg 86 - The most infamous witchcraft cases in American history were served
in Salem Massachusetts, where hundreds of people were accused of witchcraft in the late
1600s. These witchcraft cases were inspired by the voodoo lore of the Indigenous peoples just
west of Salem. In total, 19 residents were put to death, and most of these were young
adolescent women at that :(. Expanding on this, it is important to talk about who was considered
a candidate for witchcraft back then. Any women who would show a fair amount of intelligence
in comparison to her peers (women were almost never given education past primary school),
women who were independent and owned land were also considered witches, although the
accusers oftentimes were probably really just looking to take the women’s land, not accuse the
women of any superstitious behavior. Women with no children and widows were also prime
targets.
Growth of Colonial Cities pg 87 - There were cities in the colonies. These cities did in fact grow.
In fact, By the 1770s the two largest ports in Philadelphia and New York has populations of 28k

[PAGE 25]
and 25k each. Wow! Middle colonies stay winning. Cities served as trading centers and as

Commercial and Cultural Importance pg 88- Cities were the centers of industry, with more luxury
and manufactured goods being imported and exported. For example, sugar and molasses from
the Caribbean were used to create valuable rum. Culturally, new problems like Crime, pollution
and Traffic began to happen. Also Cities tended to serve as hubs and centers for new ideas
both politically and scientifically. Coffeehouses provided spaces for people to debate n stuff.

Roots of Religious Toleration pg 89 - Religious tolerance in America was at some of the best

Jeremiads pg 90 - This one has a sick title: Jeremiads were “Sermons of Despair” that were

Old Lights and New Lights pg 91 - With the cool Great Awakening, as with all new things, some

Traditional Authority Challenged pg 91 - This term refers to the Enlightenment’s competition in
the minds of Colonial Americans to the churches’. Enlightenment started to catch steam in
Europe, and so did it in the colonies, people began to see the world for how it was and decided
to derive solutions and answers from their own minds rather than a singular God or supernatural
phenomena. People began trusting their own brains more, and thus a larger interest in politics,
education, and the economy grew, with more people interested in bettering the society around
them not just through the word of God but also by the better interest of everyone around them.

High White Literacy Rates pg 92 - In America, there was an increased importance put on
Educating people and making sure they could read. Many Religious Communities created their
own schools in order to make sure people could read scripture, and many Trade Schools were
created so people could learn crafts. Many White Men were literate in the colonies, in fact over
HALF OF THEM by the Revolution could read and write. The large number of people being able
to read (like this text here) allowed for more Intellectual publications like almanacs.
Liberal Curricula pg 94 - Colleges in the colonies, such as Harvard, Yale, Princeton, Columbia,
University of Pennsylvania, College of Philadelphia, and William and Mary College just to name
a few, were basically all founded on religion and theology, more as means to train priests and
ministers. However, this did not stop many of the scholars within the institution from teaching
subjects outside of Religion, even some subjects that directly opposed it, such as some
sciences. This practice was in fact, backed and supported by the religious institutions that ran
the colleges. Some of these subjects included but are not limited to: physics, geometry,
astronomy(specifically Copernican astronomy), Rhetoric, Latin, Hebrew, Greek, mechanics,
chemistry, agriculture, government, commerce, modern languages, liberal arts, logic ethics, of
course theology, and the University of Pennsylvania was even able to open the colony's first
ever medical school

Smallpox Inoculation pg 94 - As everyone in the colonies knew due to the fact that literally like
all the indigenous people died from it, smallpox was seen as kind of a small big issue. But due
to the Enlightenment, more people were willing to try scientific experiments, like Inoculation!
Apparently some guy named Cotton Mather heard from his slave that giving people small cases
of smallpox led to immunity. So this guy tries it out, finds out it’s working in England, and
advocates for the Inoculation of people against smallpox. He did a pretty good job because by
the middle of the 18th century, Inoculation was a pretty common and widespread thing
Colonial Legal System pg 95 - Because of how far contact between the Americas and Europe
was, there was no real reason for the American colonists to follow the English law so closely. In
fact, there were many many laws that were changed in the American colonies to better suit the
needs and wants of the colonists. This is also due to the fact that there weren’t a lot of English
lawyers present, so the knowledge of the specific laws in England were scarce and hard to
come by, leading to more unique laws. This is evident in the way that criminals were punished in
the Americas, while English criminals were often sentenced to long jail times in the prisons or
gallows, American criminals often got physical beatings, whippings, floggings, branding, “the
stocks” which is basically wooden feet cuffs that were rigidly stuck together, not with chains, and
“the ducking stool”.
Colonial Governments pg 96 - Again, because of how far contact between Americans and the
Crown was, governments in colonial America kinda got to do whatever they wanted, so it
became very accustomed to the colonists to have a lot of issues be dealt with a nearly fully
self-governing body, which was oftentimes just in the community and were small assemblies of
community members. The Governors of the colonies oftentimes did not have a lot of power,
since their power came from the crown which was kind of a joke at this point, and also because

[PAGE 27]
at any moment in time, the power of the governor could be stripped away from them at the snap
of the Crown’s fingers.

Decentralized Colonial Administration pg 99 - Colonial government and law enforcement was
extremely decentralized, with no colonial office located in London to represent the colonies. In
addition, there was little to no infrastructure to actually enforce and govern the colonies. This
resulted in self-governing colonies.
Powerful Colonial Legislatures pg 100 - Colonists kinda ran their own governments, and these
governments often overpowered the Parliament and Royal authority. Colonial governments
could pass laws, levy taxes, make appropriations and appointments, and also could bypass the
governor.
Albany Plan - pg 100 Ben Franklin sees the merit in having all the colonies united against
common enemies, and having a central government for the colonies. Parliament would have
power to control Indigenous relations for the colonies and would have a leader appointed by the
king and an elected parliament. However, this idea never went through, and Ben Franklin calls
everyone a dumbass because these are the same people that want to reject Franklin’s idea of
unionizing.

Commercial VS Territorial Imperialists pg 107 The Way England viewed the colonies was
debated on heavily, and there were two main groups:
Commercial Imperialists wanted to focus on Mercantilism, trade and exports in the form of
goods
Territorial Imperialists argued that land itself was a source of income through taxation (huh) and
increased population doing work.
These two groups debated heavily after the French and Indian War, as Commercial Imperialists
wanted to negotiate for “Sugar Islands” in the Indies, while Territorial Imperialists argued for
more French Territories in the Americas.

Sugar, Currency, Stamp Acts pg 110 These are all laws passed to try and gain economic and
political control of the Colonies. All together, these laws increased taxes, gave more political
control to the crown (i.e actually dealing with smuggling), and stopped the use of Paper
currencies. The Stamp act placed a tax on every printed good in the colonies. These acts were
not received well, as they were seen as a precedent for Royal Authorities taking over the
colonies. Also they didn’t want to pay taxes, which is kind of fair.
Regulator Movement pg 110 A small scale civil war broke out as a result of the Regulator
Movement in North Carolina. The Regulators were farmers of the Carolina upcountry who
organized in opposition to the high taxes that local sheriffs collected. The western countries
were badly underrepresented in the colonial assembly, and the Regulators failed to win their
redress there. Finally, they armed themselves and began resisting by force. To suppress the
revolt, Governor WIlliam Tryon formed a group of militiamen, mostly from the eastern counties,
who beat a group of 2,000 Regulators in the Battle of Alamance. Nine on each side were killed,
and many wounded. Afterwards, six Regulators were hanged for treason. Conflicts like these
were common. The British policies about taxes and stuff eventually counterbalanced these inner
conflicts.
Paxton Boys 110 Paxton boys were a group of people from western PENNSYLVANIA that
harassed Philadelphia with demands for relief from colonial taxes and for money to defend
themselves. They were declared outlaws earlier by the Pennsylvania government after an
unauthorized attack on a neighboring indigenous tribe. Instead of surrendering, they armed
themselves and prepared to attack. Stopped by Benjamin Franklin and the government by
meeting demands.

Political Consequences of the Grenville Program pg 111 The Grenville Program was a program
set up by the British government. This program was basically a set of taxes that would be
imposed specifically on the American colonies, which included The Sugar and Stamp taxes,
both of which were massive on the American colonies. Now take a wild guess, what do you
think American colonists hated the most? That’s right: being treated like a child and also,
TAXES! Why did these taxes happen? You might ask: the French and Indian war was a costly
war that was fought by both the British and the American colonists on the same side. Despite
the camaraderie on the battlefield, it was very apparent that the British economy was in utter
shambles after the victory over the French, so to fix this, the British colonies levied taxes over
the American colonies, thinking that because the war was fought there, it would make sense
and not completely infuriate the Americans that they were being taxed. The Americans were in
fact, completely infuriated by the taxes.
Effects of the Stamp Act 112 The Colonists in America were PISSED over the Stamp Act, as
they saw it as a tax that they had no control over. In addition, since paper was a very common
item used in the colonies, this mean this tax would apply to a lot of things like Legal Documents,
Licenses, and printed documents. Colonists were afraid that the Stamp Act would set a
precedent for more goods to be taxed.
Virginia Resolves pg 113 Patrick Henry introduced a set of resolutions declaring that Americans
possessed the same rights as the English, especially the right to be taxed only by their own
representatives. Virginians should pay no taxes except those voted by the Virginia assembly,
and that anyone advocating the right of Parliament to tax Virginians should be deemed an
enemy of the colony. The House of Burgesses defeated the most extreme of these resolutions.
However, all of them were printed and circulated as the “Virginia Resolves”, implying that the
people were more militant than they were.
Sons of Liberty pg 113 A Group of Colonists who protested against Britain’s taxes in violent
ways, such as threatening tax collectors and attacking those who supported the British. In
addition, the Sons of Liberty enforced Boycotts through force.
Parliament Retreats pg 113 After much resistance to the Stamp act, a new prime minister, the
Marquis of Rockingham attempted to appease both the Americans and British by repealing the
very unpopular Stamp act, but also because the British thought that the Americans wouldn’t
follow any rules now that the Stamp act was repealed, Marquis was forced to implement the
Declaratory Act, which gave Parliament absolute authority over the colonies. This was received
pretty well at the start in the American colonies, with people celebrating the repeal of the Stamp
Act and more or less ignoring the fact that the Parliament now had 100% control over the
American colonies.
Mutiny Act pg 114 - THe Mutiny/Quartering Act of 1765 required colonists to provide quarters
(shelter) and Supplies to British Troops in America without compensation. People had already

Internal and External Taxes pg 114 British people were STILL unhappy with the way that
Rockingham (the guy that repealed the stamp act) handled the situation, saying that the
government had basically only appealed to the interest of some random hillbillies and
merchants across the Atlantic, and the King was forced to put Rockingham out of the position of

back…you guessed it; TAXES! How did William defend himself to the colonists? He argued that
Benjamin Franklin had once brought up the idea of “Internal and external” taxes. This implied
taxes that were placed on the colonies from the exterior from the Parliament and crown, and
taxes that were placed on the colonies from the interior from the colonies themselves. Uh. The
colonies didn’t buy it.
Colonial Boycotts pg 115 Boston merchants, accustomed to loose enforcement of the navigation
Acts and also angry that the new commission was trying to stop the very lucrative smuggling
trade, were indignant and took the lead in organizing another boycott. In 1768, the merchants of
Philadelphia and New York joined them, and later some southern merchants and planters also
agreed to participate. Colonists boycotted British goods subject to the Townshend Duties, and
throughout the colonies, American domestic products became fashionable, while English
luxuries fell from favor. Later in 1767(wtf we were just in 1768???) Charles Townshend suddenly
died, and the duties of dealing with colonial resistance fell to the new PM, Lord North(What a
name). Hoping to break the nonimportation agreement and divide the colonists, Lord North

Sam Adams pg 116 The leading figure in fomenting public outrage over the Boston Massacre
was none other than Samual Adams, the most effective radical in the colonies. Adams was born
in 1722 and was somewhat older than other leaders of colonial protest. As a member of an
earlier generation with strong ties to New England’s Puritan past, he was particularly inclined to
view public events in stern moral terms. A failure in business, he became a persistent voice
expressing outrage at British oppression. He argued that England had become a mass of sin
and corruption, and that only in America did public virtue survive(um…I got bad news buddy…)
IN 1772, he proposed the creation of a “committee of correspondence” in Boston to publicize
the grievances against England throughout the colony. Adams became its first head. Other
colonies followed this, and a loose network of political organizations grew up that kept the spirit
of dissent alive through the 1770s.

[PAGE 33]
England’s Balanced Constitution pg 117 England had a very balanced constitution (I have such
a way with words) (Teach me your ways!) . The system worked like this: there were 3 parts of
society, the Monarchy, the Aristocracy, and the Common People. With this system, everybody
would be easily and equally represented (In my opinion, there are many flaws, such as the fact
that the Monarchy, which would make up literally 0.00001% if not less of the population would
literally have the same power in government as 92ish% of the population, but I digress). Why
did this system come to fruition? Well, the English were very infatuated by the idea that people
were naturally evil and corrupt and selfish. To combat this, it was thought that a government
would be needed to protect each other from one’s evil, corrupt, and selfish acts. However, there
would also need to be a way to keep the government themselves from being evil, corrupt, and
selfish on top of that, so that’s how the system was made. How did this affect the Americas?
Well the Americans saw this system and were thinking to themselves: we do plenty for the
British! We make a large portion of money for them, why don’t we get a seat in this special
system? This is unfair! So yes, this is just tying into more American grievances for the British.
Virtual vs Actual Representation pg 117 So basically, the idea of Virtual Representation in
England was the idea that all members of the Parliament in England were acting on behalf of
the entire British empire. This idea led to the entirety of the English parliament basically being
nothing but full white English aristocrats, no common folk, no Indian peoples, no Irish peoples,
no American colonists, nobody of African descent, no Australians, noone from North Africa or
Islamic world. This also enraged the American colonists, since they believed the Parliament was
in need of Actual representation from American colonists to talk for American interests, since it
was proven that oftentimes Parliament did not know what the American people wanted.
Revolutionary Discourse pg 118 - Colonies also kept revolutionary sentiment alive through
writing and talking. Dissenting leaflets, pamphlets, and books were spread throughout the
colonies. In towns and cities, men gathered everywhere, especially in taverns(drunk ass
bitches) to discuss politics and express their growing distaste for English policies. The rise of
revolutionary ideology was not a result of the ideas of intellectuals, but also a product of a social
process in which ordinary people heard, discussed, and absorbed new ideas. The popular
anger lying just beneath the surface was also visible in occasional acts of rebellion. In 1772,
angry residents of Rhode Island boarded the Britiwsh(Leaving the typo in) schooner Gaspée ,
set it ablaze, and sank it in Narragansett Bay. The British response to this further angered
American opinion. Instead of putting the accused attackers on trail in colonial courts, the British
sent a special commission to America with power to send the defendants back to England for
trial. What finally revived the revolutionary fervor of the 1760s was a new act of parliament–one
that the English government had expected to be relatively uncontroversial. It involved the
business of selling tea…(Oh boy)
The Tea Act pg 118 - The Tea act made the East India Company franchise its tea out, and
made it a lot cheaper but taxed tea due to the East India Company not having to pay customs.
People saw this as the government creating a monopoly on tea with a mandatory tax tacked on
to it. This outrage would lead to massive boycotts, and overall discontent in the colonies. This
would lead to………

First Continental Congress pg 122 - In 1772, a certain Sam Adams called for delegates from all
the colonies to hold the first Continental Congress. Everybody was able to make it, besides
Georgia, but would be present in later congresses. The first congress was held in Carpenter’s
Hall in Philadelphia, where they rejected a plan to be under British authority as a colonial union.
They then endorsed a “statement of grievances” which was basically a paper complaining about
the differences between the moderates and extremists of the delegates (so basically they
recognized their differences in the subject of English rule). Third, they also planned for the
future, approving a plan to start militarizing in preparation for the possible attack of British troops
in Boston. Lastly, they agreed to stop ANY AND ALL trade between the American colonies and
Great Britain. They also agreed to meet up next Spring. So yeah. The first congress. Wahoo!
The Conciliatory Propositions pg 124 - During the winter, the Parliament in London debated
proposals for appeasing the colonists. Lord Chatham(William Pitt), the former prime minister,
urged the withdrawal of troops from America. Edmund Burke called for the repeal of the
Coercive Acts, but their efforts were in vain. Lord North won approval early in 1775 for a series
of measures known as the Conciliatory Propositions. Parliament now proposed that the
colonies, instead of being directly taxed by Parliament, would tax themselves at Parliament’s
demand. With this offer, Lord North hoped to divide the American moderates, who he thought

[PAGE 35]
represented the views of the majority. But it was too little and too late, for it did not reach
America until after the first shots of war had been fired.
General Thomas Gage pg 125 - In Boston, General Thomas Gage, commanding the British
garrison, knew of the military preparations in the countryside. He considered his army too small
to do anything until reinforcements arrived. He resisted the advice of less cautious officers, who
thought that the Americans wouldn’t dare to actually fight, and that they would back down
quickly before any show of British force. Gage was still hesitant when he received orders from
England to arrest Sam Adams and John Hancock, known to be in Lexington. But when Gage
heard that the minutemen had stored a large supply of gunpowder in Concord, he finally
decided to act. On the night of April 18th, 1775, he sent a group of about 1,000 soldiers out from
Boston on the road to Lexington. He intended to surprise the colonials and seize the supplies.
(Spoiler, it failed; “THE BRITISH ARE COMING”)
The Revolution Begins pg 126 - It was not immediately clear to either side that the skirmishes at
Lexington and Concord were the first battles of a war. Many saw them as another example of
tensions that had been afflicting Anglo-American relations for years. But whether they
recognized it at the time or not, the British and the Americans had taken a decisive step. The
War for Independence had begun.(Insert patriotic music)
Common Sense pg 130 - It’s italicized in the textbook, I don’t know why I’m wheezing. Oh nvm

written by Thomas Paine, who had emigrated from England to America fifteen months before.
He spent much of his life living in poverty, and he was ostracized because he openly ridiculed
Christianity. His pamphlets influenced the developing ideals of the United States. Much like Sam
Adams, he failed in almost all aspects of his life, but found tremendous success as a
Revolutionary. His Common Sense helped change the American outlook towards the war. Paine
wanted to turn the anger of Americans away from the specific parliamentary measures they
were resisting and redirected it toward what he considered to be the root of the problem: the
English constitution. He argued that it wasn’t enough for Americans to continue blaming their
problems on particular ministers or even the Parliament. It was the king, and the system that
allowed him to rule, that was to blame. Therefore, he said it was common sense(Now I get it) for
Americans to break from a government that could produce a king as corrupt as George III. He

The Declaration of Independence pg 130 - In 1776, a Declaration of Independence was written
by a 33 year old Thomas Jefferson with help from Ben Frank and John Adams. The declaration
honestly didn’t say anything new or heavily change the way that American life was, but it most
importantly formalized the Independence of the American colonies (obviously they still had to
fight the British though). These ideas came from town meetings, artisan and militia
organizations, County officials, juries, and the Sons/Daughters of Liberty, along with colonial
assemblies. Basically any ideas that were created by people who supported American

[PAGE 36]
patriotism would end up or at least be considered for being on the Declaration. A lot of ideas
were also inspired by the works of John Locke, a very popular philosopher at the time, such as
the idea that everybody was allowed the rights to “life”, “liberty”, and “pursuit of happiness”
(property but it doesn’t sound as cool to the average guy ). The declaration also had some
PRETTY hypocritical claims though, such as the idea that “all men are created equal”, which
was NOT reflected in the treatment of slaves, or the fact that slavery even existed at all, not to
mention that “all men” doesn’t entail women, or the fact that Jews were still discriminated
against, or the fact that after the American colonies became independent Quakers and
Anglicans were treated pretty badly, as were British loyalists. So by “All Men” it really is just
saying Patriot Free White Males. Nonetheless, the ideas in the Declaration of Independence
would make their way around the world, inspiring the French revolution as well.
Divided Americans pg 131 - After the Declaration of Independence, the colonies began to call
themselves “states”(wowee) – A reflection of their belief that each province was now, in some
ways, a separate and sovereign entity. Even before the Declaration, colonies began to operate
independently. The Parliament in London had suspended representative government in
America, but it did not end colonial self-government, instead, it increased it. The colonial
assemblies continued to meet, now free from imperial law. After the Declaration of
Independence, former colonies marked their own independence by writing formal constitutions
for themselves. By 1781, most of the new states had produced these constitutions, which
established republican governments. Some of these constitutions stood for many decades
without significant change. However, at the national level, the process of forming a government
was more halting and less successful. For a while, Americans were uncertain if they even
wanted a real national government. The Continental Congress had not been much more than a
coordinating mechanism, and virtually everyone considered the individual colonies / states the
real centers of authority. Thus began a tradition that Americans would continue for more than
two centuries: balance the commitment to state and local autonomy against the need for some
centralized authority.
Articles of Confederation pg 131 - OK! As we read more about the Independence of America
and the founding of its government, we will find ourselves looking at a LOT of important papers
that we will mix up on. Let’s get this straight: The Declaration was the American colonies giving
reasons for the American colonies to be an independent country . The Articles of Confederation
were a set of papers that confirmed the congress as the government for the newly independent
United States, but would also make it so that each state still held a majority of power in their
own matters, so the Congress was actually quite weak and had not much say over the entirety
of the colonies, it just so happened that oftentimes the colonies would agree with whatever the
Congress would say.
Financing the War pg 132 - Now that the Revolution had started, the need for money to finance
a war was high. So what did the American government do? What any good government does
during wartime: print a ton of paper money. Paper continental dollars IMMEDIATELY plummeted
to being worthless, and prices for all goods skyrocketed in the colonies (in terms of continental
dollars, which is why George Washington’s troops starved at Valley Forge in 1777, because they

[PAGE 37]
basically only had continental dollars). The American merchants were actually so fed up with the
continental dollar actually, that the American merchants and farmers began selling more to the
British, who had coins that were actually worth so much. Congress tried OVER and OVER again
to try and implement a good system to finance their war efforts, but in the end they failed and
had to resort to borrowing from other nations (France).
