Bunker Hill pg 134 - After the British withdrawal from Concord and Lexington in April of 1775,
American forces besieged the army of General Thomas Gage in Boston. The Patriots suffered
severe casualties in the Battle of Bunker Hill on June 17, 1775, and were ultimately driven from
their position there. But they inflicted much greater losses on the enemy than the enemy
inflicted on them. In fact, the British suffered their heaviest casualties of the entire war at Bunker
Hill. After the battle, the Patriots continued to tighten the siege. By the first months of 1776, the
British had concluded that Boston was not the best place to wage war. Not only was it the center
of the most Anti-British region of the colonies, it was also tactically indefensible. By late winter,
Patriot forces had surrounded the city and occupied strategic positions on the heights. On
March 17th, 1776, the British departed Boston.

Pivotal French Aid pg 140 - Basically, France big help America. The important part of note is the

with the King but also basically everybody in France allowed him to quickly get the support that

support.
Revolutionary Consequences of the Southern Campaign page 140 - The British believed that

result in their land being wrecked, so there was a reason to protect it.

Women of the Army pg 147 - Women also took part in the war, as they normally do in most wars

some would take up their husbands gear once they fell in battle to fight for the American patriots
themselves. Martha Washington for example, was a common occurrence wherever George
Washington went, and was even present at Valley Forge.

Calls for Women’s rights pg 148 - After the revolution, a growing number of women began to call

), they could own property and all that, but married women were basically 100% property to their

New Patterns of Trade pg 149 - Because the English wanted to choke the American economy

Importance of Civic Virtue pg 150 - As the new government in America slowly came together,

The success of a government based on the people relied on the nature of its people, so the idea
of a few rich people running the government was feared (HUH), and the average citizen would
ideally be an independent Land Owner.
Another part of the new American Ideology was the idea that “All men are created equal”, which
in this case meant something like a Meritocracy, where people’s skills and talents mattered
more than their social status coming from their birth or whatever. The idea was that some
people would be richer than others, but they would have to earn their success.

Statute of Religious Liberty pg 151 - Statute of Religious Liberty was a paper written by Thomas
Jefferson which completely separated church from state in Virginia. This was widely due to the
idea that many Americans had that the state should not give so much power to the church, and
even if the church were to still be part of the government, that they would not hold so much
power.

THe Ordinances of 1784 and 1785 pg 154 - Ordinances of 1784 and 1785, this one is TRICKY.
But all you really need to know for the first ordinance is that Thomas Jefferson divvied up the
Western territory that had not yet become American states into 10 territories that could all
petition for their statehood, as LONG AS, the territory had the same or larger amount of people
in the smallest state in the US at the time. This was done to avoid creating SECOND class
citizens and also to include new settlements in the Appalachian mountains in the political
structure of the nation.
Now for the NEXT ordinance in 1785, this one would create a system for surveying and selling
land. Territory North of the Ohio River would be surveyed and be marked into neat little towns
with 36 identical sections, 4 of which would belong to the American government, and 1 section
being used for a public school no matter what. Apart from that, sections could be sold off to
individuals for anything over a dollar, which is about 32$ today.

[PAGE 44]
The Grid pg 154 - Sounds cool, but the Grid refers to the system of how land was divided up for
human use. Compared to other NERDS who divided up land based on things like RESOURCES
or NATURAL BOUNDARIES, The Grid system split up land into… a grid. So like, if you got bad
land well then screw you better luck next time. This system became the standard, and became
the model form many Towns and Cities (I LOVE GRIDS MY FAVORITE STREETS 1st 2nd AND
3rd STREET)
Northwest Ordinance pg 154 - So if you remember, Thomas Jefferson created 10 separate
territories North of the Ohio river, and people were not down with this idea, settlements were too
small to hold 10 separate territories, yada yada. So instead in 1787, another ordinance was sent
out, combining all 10 territories into one Northwest Territory, which would eventually split into 3
and then 5 territories. Also, now the requirement was 60,000 people for statehood, not some
weird number dependent on the current smallest state.

troops and managed to protect his land from American settlers for a little bit, all up until the

Indigenous group of people to lose their land to the Americans, it had to be done with consent
from both parties. (People like Andrew Jackson would later find workarounds to this system via

The Founding Fathers pg 162 - Because there was such a demand for a new constitution and
centralized government, Alexander Hamilton and his supporters called for a meeting in Virginia,
where…only 5 delegates showed up. Alexander Hamilton in this meeting concluded that the
delegates should actively try to have more states show up to the next meeting to help make the
meeting actually not a joke. To do this, he actually somehow got George Washington on his
side, who agreed and basically because of him, 55 delegates from places apart from Rhode
Island showed up to Philadelphia for the next meeting. Here, these 55 dudes would be named
the Founding Fathers, and were all relatively young men, with the oldest being an 82 year old
Benjamin Franklin. The meeting did a couple things, 1, it unanimously chose George
Washington to head the meeting, and 2, it instead changed congress to a system in which not
all states, but only a majority of states would be needed for a choice to be made.
The Virginia Plan pg 162 - The Virginia Plan was a plan made by James Madison in which the
new National Legislature would actually be split into 2 houses, the lower house, which would
have states represented by its population size (basically, the bigger your state the more power
your vote holds), while the upper house members would be voted on by the lower house. Uh.

The upper house would have equal representation of all the states.
Small States versus Large States pg 163 - There was still a serious argument for having larger

The Great Compromise pg 163 - A compromise between the larger and smaller states on

with 2 people each state.
James Madison pg 164 - James Madison, is as the textbook says: the “most creative political
thinker of his generation”. James Madison helped solve 2 important questions in the newly
found American country, the question of sovereignty and the question of limiting power. Uh, the
textbook don’t say much else :|, so I will include some personal notes: James Madison helped
draft the constitution alongside Thomas Jefferson and Ben Franklin, and also helped draft the
Bill of Rights. He was also the leader of the earliest Republican party.
The Question of Sovereignty pg 165 - The question in this context, was how could the State and
National government coexist side by side? There was an obvious demand for power on either
side of the spectrum, but there was no clear line. James Madison and his followers ultimately
decided on the idea that the people should be the ultimate decider for this, and also be the
ultimate decider for the government as a whole, therefore the power of the government was not
given by the government itself, or the military, but rather by the people, hence “We the people”.
Separation of Powers pg 166 - So, the separation of powers is referring to the separation of
Legislature into 2 houses, but WHY? Well, the American government obviously did not want just
1 central power in the form of a tyranny, nor did it want a large Republic, so what would happen
would be a separation of power to multiple governing bodies, which is what the US had
established then and there and still remains today.

Federalists consisting of Alexander Hamilton, James Madison, and John Jay wrote a number of

these were the Federalists Papers.

The Bill of Rights pg 168 - Created during the first Congress, the Bill of Rights (or the first 10
Amendments) declared the rights of the people, preventing congress from infringing on certain
basic rights. Freedom of religion, speech, press, immunity from arbitrary arrest, trial by jury, and
others. The 10th Amendment reserved states all powers except those explicitly stated by the
Federal Government.
The Cabinet pg 168 - THE ROOM WHERE IT HAPPENS! (the only reason I’m doing these
parts is for the Hamilton references). The First Congress created a cabinet for the executive
department, consisting of the State, Treasury, and War departments. Hamilton is the secretary
of Treasury, Henry Knox is the Secretary of War, and Edumund Randolph is the attorney
general. Jefferson ends up as the secretary of state.
As the song goes, not everyone was happy about how this government was run, and many
were bitter about the lack of transparency and representation and stuff.
Competing Visions pg 169 - Following up on the thing above, many people with different ideas
of how the government should be run fight and debate with each other during this time. Some
believe that a Central Government is necessary for the economy and world affairs, while others
believe that American society shouldn’t be too commercial or urban, and should remain rural
and modest, with States holding more power. The people who believed in Central Government
ended up being the Federalists being lead by Hamilton, and those believing in the States
having power became the Republicans , like Thomas Jefferson
Assuming the Debt pg 169 - Hamilton proposed that the federal government should assume all
existing public debt, and pay through bonds with interest that could be paid back at a defined
date. He also suggested that the federal government should assume all debt of the States

[PAGE 48]
accrued during the Revolution. By paying through bonds, this encouraged states to have a
stake in the government succeeding, because people want to cash out on their bonds.
This plan would create a permanent “National Debt”, that would be slowly paid off and also grow
again and the cycle would repeat, putting stakes in seeing the government not completely fail.
Hamilton also wanted to create a National bank to help provide loans and also collect taxes and
stuff, since there were few banks in the country.
Hamiltion’s Report on Manufacturing pg 170 - Following up on the above thing, to fix the whole
debt problem. Hamilton proposed two taxes: ONe paid by distillers of alcohol, impacting those in
the Backcountry heavily. The other was a Tariff on imports, preventing competition from foreign
companies.
The Report itself would also lay out a plan to stimulate the growth of Industry in the US, and
also argued the advantages of his plan.
Basically a vision of how to stabilize the government, and how the nation could become wealthy
fast.
Debating Hamilton’s Program - pg 170 Few people disagreed with Hamilton’s plan for finance
and funding the national debt, but many weren’t too happy with the idea of absorbing all debt in
the nation at face value. Old War bonds had been considered worthless for a while, so buying
all these bonds back meant that the rich people who bought them off veterans and stuff would
get loaded ( TIMOTHY DEXTER W )
Location of the Capital - pg 170 - So this ties back to the Hamilton Program, which if you
remember would basically make everybody responsible for paying debt that didn’t quite affect
them (remember, people are loyal to states, not country). Because of this, Virginians in
particular were very unhappy, as they made a large portion of the population. This led to a
dinner conversation between Hamilton and Jefferson to move the capital to a new district named
Maryland in Virginia, which is how it is now.
Bank of the United States pg 170 - Hamilton really liked the idea of a national bank, and argued
that a National bank would allow for a better tax collection system, and it would make it easier
for loans to be given out to people who needed them to start businesses and so on. In 1791, the
Bank went into operation, and had the right to continue for the next 20 years.
Establishment of the Federalist Party pg 171 - Whether congress liked it or not, it was starting to
look a lot like political factions were starting to rise. In particular, the Federalist party was
already in full swing as they had earlier argued for a more centralized government and
promoted a lot of values such as big cities and industry. This was mainly headed by the current
president George Washington and a young Alexander Hamilton.

Formation of the Republican Party pg 171 - The (first, there will be another Republican party
later on) Republican party was basically an anti-Federalist party, they believed in a
decentralized government and were much more in favor of individual land owners who governed
themselves and would own large tracts of land for themselves. They didn’t like big cities, or
industry, none of that. Most Republicans came from the south, while the Federalists came from
primarily the North. This was headed by Thomas Jefferson and James Madison.
Differences over the French Revolution pg 172 - As the French Revolution came into full swing,
opposing parties in the US found themselves reacting in completely different ways. The
Federalists were in horror of the execution of the king and queen and overthrowing of the
monarchy in France, while the Republicans were in wide support of the overthrow, and even
started to refer to one another as “citizens” and “citizenesses”, basically one side didn’t like the
revolution, and one side was big fans of it. Not that complicated.

The QuasiWar pg 176 - (A unoffical naval war between the Fr*nch and US) Adams persuaded
Congress to cut off all trade with France and to authorize American vessels to capture French
armed ships on the high seas. In 1798, Congress created a Department of the Navy and
appropriated money for the construction of new warships. The navy soon won a number of
duels with French vessels and captured 85 ships, including armed merchantmen. The US also
began cooperation closely with the British and became an ally of Britain in the war with
France(How the turntables)
In the end, France chose to conciliate before the conflict grew.
Alien and Sedition Acts pg 176 - The conflict with France helped the Federalists increase their
majorities in Congress in 1798. With this new strength, they began to consider ways to silence

[PAGE 51]
the Republican opposition. The result was some of the most controversial legislation in
American history: the Alien and Sedition Acts. The Alien Act placed new obstacles in the way of
immigrants, and strengthened the president’s hand in dealing with these immigrants. The
Sedition Act allowed the government to prosecute those who engaged in sedition (speech
encouraging people to rebel against the government) against the government. In theory, only
libelous or treasonous activities were subject to prosecution; but since such activities were open
to widely varying definitions, the law made it possible for the federal government to stifle almost
any opposition.

Virginia and Kentucky Resolutions pg 177 - Republican leaders who believed that states had the
power to nullify congressional legislation laid out a theory for state action in two sets of
resolutions in 1798-1799. The Kentucky Resolution was written anonymously by Jefferson and
the Virginia one was drafted by Madison. These Resolutions used the ideas of John Locke to
argue that the federal government had been formed by a contract among the states and
possessed only certain delegated powers. Whenever the federal government exercised any
undelegated powers, its acts were voided. The Kentucky resolution claimed that they had the
right to nullify the appropriate laws if the parties to the states determined that the central
government had exceeded those powers.
BASICALLY: Republicans didn’t want the ALien and Sedition acts anymore. They think that the
states should have the right to judge federal laws and that acts laid down by the gov that were
too extreme would be void and of no force.
The Election of 1800 pg 177 - YEAH YEAH THIS WAS THE SHIT THAT GOT HAMILTON
KILLED!!! So basically, Adams and Jefferson were competing for the spot of president, and a
whole bunch of drama happened. Strap in boys, because shit’s about to get wild. The campaign
was an ugly one, while the candidates displayed dignity, their supporters were going bonkers.
The Federalists accused Jefferson of being a dangerous radical and his followers of being wild
men who would bring on a reign of terror comparable to that of the French Revolution. The
Republicans portrayed Adams as a tyrant conspiring to become king, and accused the
Federalists of plotting to subvert human liberty and impose slavery on the people. There were
also considerable personal insults as well. For example, it was during this campaign that the
story of Jefferson’s romantic involvement with a slave woman on his plantation was first widely
aired. It was close, and the crucial contest was in New York. There, Aaron Burr(Sir) had
mobilized an organization of Revolutionary War veterans, the Tammany Society, to serve as a
Republican political machine. And through Tammany’s efforts, the Republicans carried the city
by a large majority, and with it, the state. Jefferson was, apparently, elected. But an unexpected
complication soon jeopardized the Republican victory. This is where it gets good. So each
person now had to vote for two people, instead of just voting for the presidential candidate and
for the vice presidential candidate. To avoid a tie, Republicans came up with a weird plan where
only one person will not vote for Burr. Of course, the plan failed. Jefferson and Burr tied, and a
long ballot process ensued. Finally, Hamilton casted the deciding vote in favor of Jefferson,
because he thought Burr to be untrustworthy.

The Jeffersonian Vision pg 181 Jefferson viewed America as a society of independent farmers
free from Industry and the Urban Sprawls of Europe. He would promote localism and simplicity,
with a weakened central government.
BASICALLY Anti-Fed stuff
However in reality, America was developing in the opposite direction with more and more cities
and flowing commerce and industrialism. Jefferson helped weaken the Federal government, but
he also found himself in situations where a Central government was necessary for the function
of the country.
Importance of Virtuous Citizenry pg 181 Jefferson wanted all the Citizens of America to be
educated. He believed that all white male citizens should be educated for free. Some states
endorsed free education, but nobody really followed through. Schooling would be something for
private institutions more of the time than not.
Private Schooling pg 181 Most of the time. Only those who could afford education would you
know, be educated. Many Private schools are run by religious groups, and end up with a mostly
aristocratic school population training to become the nation’s elite
NEw Educational Opportunities for Women pg 182 Before, women often did not get educated at
all, and at least 50% of women were illiterate in the colonies. However, people started thinking
“how can we have educated kids if the people raising kids aren’t educated?” and this brilliant
line of thinking led to the creation of Female academies throughout the nation. In addition, more
Feminist ideals start to appear as women argue for the rights to education and more
independence.
Higher Education pg 182 - higher education was less widely available than education at lower
levels, despite Republican hopes for a wide dispersion of advanced knowledge.(Jefferson
formed the University of Virginia to promote that ideal.) The number of colleges and universities
in America grew from nine at the start of the Revolution to a whopping twenty-two, and
continued to increase. None of these new schools was truly public. Even those established by
state legislatures relied on private contributions and on tuition fees. Scarcely more than one
white man in a thousand (and no women, African Americans, or Indigenous) had access to
college education.

[PAGE 53]
Indian Education pg 182 - Reformers who believed in the power of education to reform and
redeem ignorant and “backward” people spurred a growing interest in Indigenous education.
Because Jefferson and his followers liked to think of Native Americans as “noble savages”, they
hoped that schooling the indigenous in white culture would tame and “uplift” the tribes. Although
the governments did little to promote Indigenous education, missionaries and mission schools
increased rapidly among the tribes.
Almost no one thought that there was a need to educate African Americans.
Benjamin Rush pg 183 A Philadelphia physician that figured out that sanitation was actually
important for hospitals and doctors . This was an extremely revolutionary and new idea that
helped stop disease.
He still took some Ls though - he believed in bleeding as a medical solution - which would

Decline of Midwifery pg 183 - In the early nineteenth century, physicians began to handle
deliveries themselves and to demand restrictions on the role of midwives. Among the results of
that change was a narrowing of opportunities for women(midwifery was an important female
occupation) and a restriction of access to childbirth care for poor mothers.
Establishment of a National Culture pg 183 - Many Americans in the Jeffersonian era may have
rejected the Federalist belief in political and economic centralization, but most embraced
another form of nationalism with great passion. Having won political independence from Europe,
they now aspired to a form of cultural independence.In the process, they wanted American
literary and artistic life that could rival those in Europe. As a popular “Poem on the Rising Glory
of America” had predicted as early as 1772, Americans believed that their “happy land” was
destined to become the “seat of empire” and the final stage of civilization.
Noah Webster pg 184 - Webster is the guy who insisted on a more simple spelling(Color instead
of colour). His American Spelling Book , first published in 1783 and commonly known as the
“blue-backed speller,” eventually sold over 100 million copies. It became the best-selling book
(except for the Bible) in the history of American publishing. Also, his school dictionary, issued in
1806, was republished in many editions and was eventually enlarged to become An American
Dictionary of the English Language in 1828. His speller and his dictionary established a national
standard of words and usages.
Washington Irving pg 184 - Washington Irving was a resident of New York State and won wide
acclaim for his satirical histories of early American life and powerful tales of society in the New
World. His popular folklores, narrating the adventures of American agrarians like Ichabod Crane
and Rip Van Winkle (I need to hire this guy to name my kid) made him “critically acclaimed”
leader of American literary life in his era and one of the few writers of that time whose books
would be continued to be read by later generations.

[PAGE 54]
Deism pg 184 - A “religion” that accepted the existence of God, but considered God a remote
being who withdrew from common affairs after creating the universe.(So pretty much atheists)
Books and articles attacking religious “superstitions” attracted wide readership and induced lots
of discussions, and among them was Thomas Paine’s The Age of Reason , published in parts
from 1794 to 1796. Paine once called Christianity the “strangest religion ever set up,” because
“it committed a murder upon Jesus in order to redeem mankind from the sin of eating an apple”.
Cane Ridge pg 185 - By 1800, the revivalist energies of all the sects were combining to create
the greatest surge of evangelical fever since the first Great Awakening(Wow who’d have thunk
it! It’s almost like it’s the SECOND Great awakening! OH WAIT, IT IS!!!!!) Beginning among
Presbyterians in several eastern colleges, it soon spread rapidly throughout the country,
reaching its greatest heights in the western regions. In just a few years, a large portion of the
American people were mobilized by the movement, and membership in churches that embraced
the revival, like the Methodists, the Baptists, and the Presbyterians, skyrocketed. At Cane
Ridge, a group of evangelical ministers presided over the nation’s first “camp meeting” , an
extraordinary revival that lasted several days and impressed all who saw it with its size. (An
estimated 25,000 people attended)
Message of the Second Great Awakening pg 185 - Basically, individuals must re-admit God and
Christ (religion yippee) into their daily lives, have to embrace an intense and active piety, and
must reject the rationalism that threatened traditional beliefs. The Second Great Awakening
failed to revive the religious ideas of the past. Few of the revivalist sects still accepted the idea
of predestination; and the belief that a person could change his or her own destiny added more
fuel to the individual’s search for salvation. In short, the Awakening combined a more active
piety with a belief in God as an active force in the world. It also accelerated the growth of
different sects. It helped create a broad popular acceptance of the idea that men and women
could belong to different Protestant churches and still be committed to essentially the same
Christian faith.

Eli Whitney’s Cotton Gin pg 189: The cotton gin was an amazing invention that removed the
seeds out of the cotton, which was really difficult to do, and usually took many hours of skilled,
and laborious work just to produce 3 pounds of cotton in a day. (The gin in it’s name is an
abbreviation of engine apparently). The machine was a simple machine which contained a
toothed roller that caught the fibers of the cotton and pulled them between the wires of a grating.
The grating then caught the seeds while a revolving brush removed the lint from the rollers
teeth. With this invention, a single operator could clean as much cotton in a few hours as a
group of workers cleaning cotton for a whole day.
The Cotton Gin’s Impact on the NORTH pg 189: As cotton became easier to harvest, demand
went up, and many entrepreneurs in New England and elsewhere had strong incentives to start
a textile business. In the Northern states, it was pretty hard to do agricultural business but by
learning how to turn cotton into yarn and thread, plantation owners could make tons of money in
the industrial industry instead. But with this emerging industrial textile movement in the North,
there was a wedge driven between the nations two most populous regions, one becoming
increasingly industrial, while the other still being very rooted in agriculture.

Rapid Growth of American Shipping pg 189: There were multiple ways to solve the problem of
the small American market. One of these ways was to look for customers overseas. Among the
first acts of the new Congress when it met in 1789 were two tariff (Tariff and Collection Act) bills
giving preference to American ships in American ports; stimulating an expansion of domestic
shipping. More importantly was the wars that broke out over Europe in the 1790s, allowing
American merchant vessels to carry most of the trade between Europe and the Western
Hemisphere. Very early on, America had a merchant marine and a booming foreign trade
economy. (Second only to England) The United States had more ships and international
commerce per capita than any other country.

The Turnpike era pg 190 In 1792, a corporation constructed a toll road running the sixty miles
from Philadelphia to Lancaster. This road proved so successful that several other companies
laid out similar turnpikes (named for the kind of tollgate that was frequently used) from other
cities to neighboring towns. These roads had to be profitable, so they were only made in short
distances and through densely populated areas.
Urban Life pg 191 People living in towns and cities lived differently than the vast majority of
Americans who continued to work as farmers. Urban life produced affluence, amongst other
things. Affluent people often sought amenities that were so luxurious, they could only be used
by the wealthy farmers. They sought increasing elegance and refinement in their homes, their
properties, and their clothes. They also looked for activities–music, theater, dancing, and, most
popularly, horse racing.

Judicial Review pg 195 Federalists had long maintained the view that the Supreme Court had
the authority to nullify acts of Congress(despite the fact that the Constitution said nothing about
it), and the Court had exercised the power of judicial review in 1796 when it upheld the validity
of a law passed by the legislature. The Court’s authority in this area would not be secure until it
actually declared a congressional act unconstitutional.
Marbury V Madison pg 195 In 1803, in the case of Marbury v. Madison , it did so. William
Marbury, one of Adams’s “midnight appointments,” had been named a justice of the peace in the
District of Columbia. But his commission had not been delivered to him before Adams left office.
Once Jefferson became president, James Madison, the new secretary of state, refused to had
the commission to Marbury, even though it was already signed and sealed by Adams. Marbury
appealed to the Supreme Court for an order directing Madison to do his duty. In the ruling, the
Court found that Marbury had a right to his commission but it ordered that Madison had no
authority to deliver it. In the end, Marbury never got his commission.
TL;DR In 1803, the case of Marbury v. Madison happened. William Marbury had been chosen
as a judge by President Adams, but his official papers (commission) weren’t given to him before
Adams left office. When Jefferson became president, James Madison refused to give Marbury
the papers. Marbury asked the Supreme Court to make Madison hand them over. The Court
said Marbury should have gotten his commission, but also said Madison didn’t have the power
to give it to him. So in the end, Marbury never got the job.
John Marshall pg 196 Ok here's a funny thing: John Marshall, before becoming chief justice of
the Supreme Court, was Adams's secretary of state. Mr.Marshall was actually the one who
didn’t want to give Marbury the commission. Anyway, John Marshall, immediately after being
appointed to chief justice, established himself as the most dominant figure on the Court, shaping
all of its most important rulings. Through a succession of Republican administrations, he
established the judiciary as a branch of government coequal with the executive and the
legislative–a position that the founders of the republic never clearly indicated it should occupy.

[PAGE 58]
TL;DR Marshall made the Judiciary branch as a dominant figurehead between the Executive
and Legislative branches, which is what the founding fathers didn’t want. Dude was Adams’s

The Napoleonic Wars pg 202 Later in Thomas Jefferson’s presidency, two very different
conflicts were taking shape. They would draw the United States into a difficult and frustrating
war. One was the continued tension in Europe, which escalated in 1803 into yet another

[PAGE 60]
full-scale conflict. The Napoleonic Wars saw both England and France taking steps to prevent
the US from trading with the other.

America’s Predicament pg 203 American ships were caught between Napoleon’s decrees and
Britain’s orders in council. If they sailed directly for the European continent, they risked being
captured by the British navy, and if they sailed for British ports, then they risked seizure from the
Fr*nch. Both of the nations were violating America’s rights as a neutral nation. Most Americans
considered the British, with their greater navy, the worst of the two.
Chesapeake-Leopard Incident pg 203 In the summer of 1807, the British went to even more
extreme measures in an incident involving a vessel of the American navy. Sailing for NOrfolk,
with “deserters” from the British navy among the crew, the American naval frigate Chesapeake
encountered the British ship Leopard . When the American commander, James Barron, refused
British access to their ship, the Leopard opened fire. Barron had to surrender, and a boarding
party dragged four men off the frigate. Obviously, this caused immense outrage in America, and
if congress was in session, it might have declared war.
The Embargo pg 204: It was basically an act that stopped American trade in foreign ports. It
was signed by Jefferson in December of 1807. France passed a law that stopped trade between
countries like the U.S. and Britain. In simpler terms, it closed U.S. ports to all exports and
restricted imports from Britain as an attempt to avert war. It was widely evaded but still hit the
nation hard, putting the US in a depression of some sort. The hardest hit were the merchants
and shipowners of the Northeast, most of them Federalists.
Non-Intercourse Act pg 204: AyOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOO
Apparently it’s actually a law passed to replace the embargo act and it reopened trade with all
nations BUT Britain and France. A year after it was enacted, congress allowed it to expire and
replaced it again with Macon’s Bill No.2 which reopened trade with Britain and France but under
conditions. Napoleon announced that France would no longer interfere w/ American shipping
and low Madison announced that unless Britain renounced its restrictions on American shipping,
they would make an embargo against Britain. Eventually, the British government repealed its
blockade of Europe but it was too late to prevent the war.
William Henry Harrison pg 205: William Henry Harrison was a 26 year old Virginian with veteran
experience in fighting Indigenous peoples and taking their land, and a congressional delegate in
1799 representing the Northwest Territory(remember, these areas aren’t quite states just yet).
So what did this guy DO? Harrison implemented the Harrison Land Law in 1800, which

[PAGE 61]
gave White settlers easier access to the newly acquired American territory than before ,
how was this done you might ask? Well, Harrison made the price of buying Western land halved
right out the gate, but you would still have to pay full price by making sure you paid the rest of
the price over the course of 4 years. Why did this work? Put simply, buying land West was a
little too expensive in the past, since you would hafta pay so much upfront, the new law allowed
for more middle-class Americans looking to pursue new environments and easier access to land
west of the Eastern coast.

Postwar Issues pg 215 If you remember, the war of 1812 cut off imports for a while, which did
help the domestic manufacturing industry, but also brought with it a lot of chaos in the shipping
and banking industry for obvious reasons. This also exposed the fact that American
infrastructure for trade was just straight up awful, although the textbook doesn’t say why :)). The
important thing to note for this term is that the aftermath of the war left the country in a tough
financial spot, and made the American government focus once again on its economy.
Second Bank of the United States pg 215 In 1811, the first Bank of the US had its charter
expire, and a large number of state banks started opening up, and although these were popular,
the banks didn’t have enough money to really back the notes up. Another problem with these
state banks was that there were a lot of different bank notes in circulation that were not super
easily traced back to their origins, meaning that there was yet another currency problem in the
US, with confusion and counterfeiting everywhere(you could just say “uh… this bank note is
from xyz state haha, give me money). In order to combat this, Congress chartered the second
Bank of the US in 1816, and was basically the exact same bank that Hamilton founded in 1791
but it had more capital and was more heavily sponsored. Even though it had no power to issue
currency, its size allowed it to be virtually everywhere, and it put other banks out of business.
Growth of the Textile Industry pg 216 Because of the US government shutting down imports, the
American manufacturing industry boomed, with the textile industry in particular leading the way
between 1807 and 1815. This was not only because of the aforementioned factor, but also
because of the invention of the Cotton Gin, which made domestic production of cotton absurdly
high. Francis Cabot Lowell in 1813 created a super powerful loom that was a lot more efficient
than its English counterpart, and also founded the first mill in America that was meant for
spinning and weaving at the same time.
A Protective Tariff pg 216 In 1816, a group named “protectionists” in Congress managed to pass
a Tariff law that limited FOREIGN competition in American domestic markets, with cotton cloth
being the one that was most limited, this would sponsor more domestic competition and would
hopefully raise the strength of the American economy. Doesn’t really say the effects of this tariff
at all, which is a very APUSH textbook thing to do. :/

[PAGE 65]
Government Funded Roads pg 217 AMERICA ROAD BAD! This was the sentiment that a lot of
people had, despite turnpikes and the steamboat, there was still an issue with the fact that there
weren’t any roads that were recognized by the government, making it harder for people to travel
throughout the American east coast. Jefferson and his secretary Albert Gallatin proposed that
revenues from the Ohio land sales should be used to finance a National Road from the Potomac
River to the Ohio River. This was quickly approved by congress and in 1811, it went into
construction, and by 1818 the highway was finished. These quickly became pretty traffic filled
roads, and along with the Lancaster turnpike, the transportation issue was one step closer to
being solved, and the domestic market for goods, specifically textiles and cotton grew even
larger than before.
Steamboats pg 217 Robert Fulton’s steamboat was a major success and unlike the roads on
land, they didn’t need much work. The War of 1812 obviously halted the development of trade in
the rivers of the US, but once that was all solved, steamboats would travel up and down the
rivers carrying cargo, and helped the agricultural trade between the West and the South to the
East .
Vetoing Internal Improvements pg 218 As we discussed, the economy of the US was still kind of
not doing so hot, mainly because of a lack of infrastructure. It was apparent when the British
blockaded American trade in the Atlantic, which greatly stifled the American economy, and there
was a demand for this issue to be fixed. Representative John C Calhoun proposed a bill that
would use funds owed to the government by the Bank to be instead used to fix internal
problems. Seems great right? Presiden Madison would agree, but he vetoed the bill, why? He
argued that congress simply didn’t have the power or money to do such a thing, and argued that
state governments and private enterprises should be the ones to fix these issues instead.
Reasons for Westward Expansion pg 218 Put simply, the US had way too much to gain by
moving west. By moving west, not only did the US gain obviously more land, but they also
diversified the economy while simultaneously improving it since it cost so little to fund the

Calhoun’s Theory of Nullification 236 Calhoun’s Theory of Nullification was presented as a
possible solution to the issue of state succession. Inspired by the Virginia and Kentucky
resolutions, along with the 10th Amendment, John C. Calhoun, a 46 year old former
protectionist, argued that because the Federal government was created by the states, it was up
to the states to decide whether certain ones could determine whether or not certain policies
were constitutional or not. How this was done was by allowing states to veto certain Federal
laws and policies specifically for that state. This idea caught fire in South Carolina, but didn’t
help much with John’s popularity since Calhoun had a bigtime rival in Martin Van buren

The Seminole War (again) 241 The Seminoles in Florida were the only tribe to resist the
pressures to relocate, and even their success was limited. Like other tribes, the Seminoles had
agreed, under pressure, to a settlement by which they ceded their lands to the government and
agreed to move to a reservation within three years. (The 1832-1833 treaties of Payne’s
Landing). Most did move, but a substantial minority, under the leadership of Osceola, refused to
leave and staged an uprising. A group of runaway black slaves joined the Seminoles in their
struggle. This war went on for years, Jackson’s troops did little to suppress the uprising due to
the guerrilla warfare tactics the Seminoles and their African American associates employed.
Even after Osceola had been captured while under a flag of truce and died in prison, and even
after white troops had engaged in a systematic campaign of extermination against the resisting
Indigenous and their black allies, followers of Osceola remained in Florida. Finally, in 1842, the
government abandoned the war. By then, most of the Seminoles had been either killed or forced

[PAGE 74]
out west. But the relocation of the Seminoles,unlike other tribes, was never complete.
Something to note: the Seminole community in Florida has made a big rebound, and it is clear
that despite only a small number of Seminole survivors in the Florida area, they managed to
thrive in the area despite the wet marshes and harsh heat.

Jackson’s opposition to Concentrated Power 242
Jackson was willing to use federal power against States and against Native people, but was
very against concentrating economic power in the Federal Government and the “aristocratic
institutions” around it (the national bank) .
One example of Jackson’s view on Federal Power came when he vetoed a measure to pay for a
road in Kentucky. Jackson argued that this would solely benefit one state, because the road was
in Kentucky and not part of “interstate commerce”. He also saw paying for the road in general as
extravagant and unnecessary.

Hard Money people believed that money was only valuable because it was backed by gold and

Jackson’s Veto 242 Clay, Webster, and other advisers persuaded Biddle to apply to Congress in
1832 for a bill to renew the Bank’s charter. That was four years ahead of the date the original
charter was scheduled to expire. Forcing a vote now would allow the Bank to become a major
issue in the 1832 national elections. Congress passed the recharter bill; Jackson, predictably,
vetoed it’ and the Bank’s supporters in Congress failed to override the veto. Just as Clay had
hoped, the 1832 campaign now centered on the future of the Bank.
Removal of Government Deposits 243 Determined to destroy the “monster” Bank, Jackson tried
to weaken it. He removed the government’s deposits from the Bank. His secretary of treasury
believed that such an action would destabilize the financial system and refused to do so, so
Jackson fired him. He then hired a new one, but this new secretary didn’t want to give the order
either, so Jackson again fired him. For his third secretary, Jackson hired Attorney General Roger
B. Taney, his close friend and loyal ally. Taney began placing the government’s deposits not in
the Bank of the United States, but in a number of state banks.
Jackson Victorious 243 Jackson had won a considerable political victory when he got rid of the
federal bank. This led to a fragmented and unstable banking system that would continue for a
century.

Charles River Bridge V Warren Bridge 244 This case involved a dispute between two
Massachusetts companies over the right to build a bridge across the Charles River between
Boston and Cambridge. One company had a long-standing charter from the state to operate a
toll bridge and claimed that this charter guaranteed it a monopoly of the bridge traffic. Another
company had applied to the legislature for authorization to construct a second, competing
bridge that would–since it would be toll free–greatly reduce the value of the first company’s
charter. In the end, Taney(idk his first name), speaking for the Democratic majority, allowed the
second bridge to be built. His reasoning was that states had the right to amend or abrogate a
contract if it is necessary to advance the well-being of the community. This decision reflected
one of the cornerstones of the Jacksonian ideal: that the key to democracy was an expansion of
economic opportunity, which would not occur if older corporations could maintain monopolies
and choke off competition from new companies.
SUMMARY: Charles River Bridge V Warren Bridge - Dispute over two Massachusetts
companies over the right to build a bridge across the Charles River between Boston and
Cambridge. Charles River Bridge Company claimed a monopoly based on its charter while
Warren Bridge Company wanted to build a free bridge. Chief Justice Taney ruled in favor of
Warren Bridge arguing that states could change contracts to benefit the public. It reflects
Jacksonian ideals of promoting economic opportunity and opposing monopolies.
Birth of the Whig Party 244 Jackson’s forceful, even tyrannical tactics in crushing the nullification
movement and then the Bank of the United States helped galvanize a growing opposition
coalition that by the mid-1830s was ready to assert itself in national politics. Denouncing the

[PAGE 76]
president as “King Andrew I,” they began to refer to themselves as Whigs, after the party in
England that had traditionally worked to limit the power of the king.
Democrats’ Emphasis on Opportunity 245 Democrats in the 1830s envisioned a future of
steadily expanding economic and political opportunities for white males(hmm i wonder why…).
They believed that the role of government should be limited, but it should include efforts to
remove obstacles to opportunity and to avoid creating new ones. This meant defending the
Union, which Jacksonians believed was essential to the dynamic economic growth they favored.
It also meant attacking centers of corrupt privilege, as Jackson himself said in his farewell
address, the society of America should be one in which “the planter, the farmer, the mechanic,
and the laborer, all know that their success depends on their own industry and economy.”
Summary: In the 1830s, democrats believed that white men should have growing economic and
political opportunities. They wanted a limited government that focused on removing obstacles to
success instead of creating obstacles. They supported preserving the union for economic
growth and opposite wealthy elite (corrupt privilege). In his farewell address, Jackson said
America should be a place where farmers and workers succeed through hard work
Whigs’ Call for Economic Union 245 The political philosophy that became known as Whiggery
was very different. It favored expanding the power of the federal government, encouraging
industrial and commercial development, and knitting the country together into a consolidated
economic system. Whigs embraced material progress, but unlike the Democrats, they were
cautious about westward expansion., fearing that it would create instability. Their vision of
America was of a nation embracing the industrial future and rising to world greatness as a
commercial and manufacturing power.
Whigs - different view. They wanted a strong fed gov, wanting industry, commerce and a
connected national economy. They supported economic progress but were cautious about
westward expansion because it would cause problems. Their goal - America = powerful,
industrial and commercial nation.
Anti-Masons 245 Both the Whig and Democratic politicians made frequent adjustments in their
public postures to attract the largest possible number of voters. For example, the Whigs in New
York worked to develop a popular following by making a connection to a movement known as
Anti-Masonry. The Anti-Masonic movement emerged in the 1820s in response to widespread
resentment against the secret, exclusive, and thus undemocratic Society of Freemasons. These
resentments grew when a former Mason, WIlliam Morgan, mysteriously disappeared(assumed
to be murdered) from his home in New York shortly before he was going to publish a book on
the secrets of Freemasonry.
Both the Whigs and Democrats often changed their positions to gain more voters. In New York,
the Whigs gained support by aligning with Anti-Masonic movement which began in the 1820s.
This movement opposed the secretive and exclusive Freemasons, who were seen as
undemocratic—especially after a former Mason, William Morgan, disappeared before revealing
their secrets in a book.
Cultural Issues 245 Religious and Ethnic Differences factored heavily into people’s party
preferences. Immigrants like Irish and German Catholics supported Democrats and their

[PAGE 77]
aversion to commercial development and entrepreneurial progress. They also favored the
Democrats’ family/community focused values.
Protestants favored the Whigs because of their constant development and improvement.
The Whigs would end up being more successful at defining its values and therefore attracting a
following. However the Whigs did not really have a leader and instead had 3 main guys that
people considered a group of leaders, consisting of Henry Clay, Daniel Webster, and John
Calhoun . Sound familiar? These guys have popped up here and there in the past couple of
terms.
Clay’s American System 245 Clay won support from many of those who favored his program for
internal improvements and economic development, what he called the “American System”. But
his image as a devious operator and his identification with the West proved to be serious
liabilities. He ran for president three times and never won(yikes).
Election of 1836 pg 246 The problems that emerged from this divided leadership became
particularly clear in 1836. The Democrats were united behind Jackson’s pick, Martin Van Buren,
but the Whigs couldn’t agree on a candidate. Instead, they sent three in hopes of gaining a
majority for their party. However, this plan failed, as Van Buren won with 170 electoral votes to
124 for all his opponents combined.
Distribution Act pg 246 Congress and the administration now faced the question of what to do
with the surplus Treasury. Reducing the tariff was not an option, as no one wanted to go through
that again. Instead, support grew for returning the federal surplus to the states. In 1836,
Congress passed a “distribution” act requiring the federal government to pay its surplus funds to
the states each year in four quarterly installments as interest-free, unsecured loans.
Panic of 1837 247 WAS THAT THE PANIC OF 37?????
In 1836, before 8th president Martin Van Buren came into office, Andrew Jackson issued an
order that would require all payment for public lands would only be paid in gold/silver or
currency backed by gold/silver. (NO WAY THIS IS LIKE THE SILVER DRAIN FROM AP
WORLD HISTORY)
This created an economic panic, where many banks and businesses failed, Unemployment
grew massively, and riots broke out in cities over the price of goods like Bread.
The Prices of things dropped massively, and many public works projects like Bridges failed.
Many States couldn’t pay interest on their bonds, and it caused the worst Depression in
American History at this point . What made this worse was the fact Europe was also having
some economic problems, so many European countries pulled their American Investments out.
This whole cluster of problems happened during Van Buren’s Democratic administration, and

government intervention in the economies. The things Van Buren did do were very

[PAGE 78]
inconsequential, such as taking money to pay government debts and establishing a 10 hour
workday on all federal projects.
Van Buren would also end up creating a new financial system for the US, one that would be a bit
controversial…
Independent Treasury pg 247 Van Buren’s plan, called the “independent treasury” or
“subtreasury” system, was the most important and controversial of all of Van Buren’s legislative
achievements. It placed government funds in an independent treasury in Washington and in sub
treasuries in other cities. No private banks would have the government’s money or name to use
as a basis for speculation; the banks and the government would be “divorced’. This plan failed
to pass in the House, but in the last year of his presidency, it was finally passed by Congress.
New Techniques of Political Campaigning 248 The 1840 campaign was the first in which the
new “penny press” carried news of candidates to a large audience of workers. It also showed
how the concept of party competition and the subordination of ideology to immediate political
needs had been fully established in the US. The Whigs represented the affluent elements of the
population, and favored policies that aided businesses. Yet they presented themselves in 1840
as the party of the common people, and so did the democrats. Both parties used the same
techniques of mass voter appeal, and, in the end, the Whigs won. The 1840 campaign was the
first to use the "penny press" to reach many working-class voters. It showed that party
competition often mattered more than ideology. Both Whigs and Democrats claimed to
represent the common people, even though the Whigs supported the wealthy and business
interests. Using techniques such as slogans, songs, rallies and penny press, they won!

Regional Divergences 255 As we know already, the Northern and Southern states are pretty
different. However, around the 1800s, these differences started to really show and become
important. The Northern states developed industrial economies dominated by cities and
industry. In the South, states focused on resources and agriculture in cash crops. Crops like
cotton became important to the economies, as Northern Textile factories needed cotton to well,
make textiles. The South remained much less developed economically, and the southern states
became more and more reliant on Slavery to keep up with production to support the North.

Impact of the Market Economy 268 By the middle years of the 19th Century(1840s-1860s?) was
the start of the development of a modern capitalist economy. The emergence of this new
economy allowed an increase in wealth across the nation, but this didn't affect every place
equally because some classes and regions benefited more.
Advantages of the Corporation 268 Due to an increase in capitalist merchants and also the
many laws passed to sponsor business and corporations given by states, such as giving
charters to companies for just a small fee. New laws also made investing a lot more risk-free.
This was done through a limited-liability policy in which investors only lost the money they put
into investment, rather than more than they invested in companies.
Inadequate Credit 268 In order to fulfill the financial needs of these up and coming corporations
and businesses, corporations needed credit alongside individual investment since investors
alone couldn’t pay for much. Many state governments had pretty shitty credit systems that didn’t
help much or did not guarantee money that is to be loaned to either be paid back or paid in the
first place, and as a result banks turned to issuing a lot of bank notes. Why is this ‘inadequate’?
Well because when you have banks not issuing actual money and instead issuing a bunch of
‘notes’ that simply secure money that might not actually be available, what ends up happening
is depressions, in which people who expect their money don’t get that money. When people are
given a guarantee or promise of money, they tend to spend a little more than what they normally
do leading up to that promise of money, resulting in economic depression. Such is the case with
the Roaring 20s leading up to the Great Depression. This is why it is important to have a strong
credit and currency system, along with working banks.

Transformation of American Agriculture 270 America’s agricultural technology and industry
began to boom as 90% of the American population was basically just farmers. More importantly,
these farmers were moving West into new territories with new land. This increased popularity in
farming led to an increase in farming technology, technique, infrastructure, and transportation
systems, all contributing to a massive increase in food production in the US. In addition, thanks
to the increased transportation, the US people no longer had to rely on food grown from their
own homes, and instead could import food domestically from Western states, and also import
from other countries along with their existing exports.

Rise of Commercial Agriculture 288 Agriculture as an industry became more and more like an
industry tied to American capitalism (if that doesn’t make sense, you can think of it as how the
tech market in California makes up a big portion of the Calfornian economy over manufacturing
in the California economy). Basically what this meant was that the agriculture market would
work in some parts of the US super well, and other parts not so well.
Truck Farming in the Northeast 288 Let’s get a little context out of the way: agriculture economy
in the Northeast is dead in the water. The Soil there is ass and there is no reason to farm there
when you can move to the Northwest and make so much more money there for half the cost.
This resulted in the “truck farming” economy in the Northeast. Because Northeastern folks saw
no reason to farm tobacco or cotton(cash crops) like they used to, they instead turned to
supplying cities with food, growing vegetables and fruit instead to be sold locally.
Industrialization in the Old Northwest 288 Because of the drop in agricultural markets in the
Northeast, most of the northeast started to go into industry. Some 209,909 workers were
employed in the North by 1860, and the number kept growing. Cleveland became an incredible
industrial center, as did Cincinnati with its meatpacking industry. But most importantly, Chicago
became the great metropolis of the region, with both agricultural machinery and meatpacking.
Agricultural Specialization 289 As said before, the agricultural industry in America became more
and more included in American capitalism, and as capitalism does, capitalism promotes
competition. Farmers in the Northwest(remember, Northeast no more farming) began to focus
on farming specific crops for profits, whether that be a crop like corn, wheat, or any sort of
livestock. (fun fact, corn is in 75% of all of our processed food, the only reason why Indiana
exists)

McCormick Reaper 289 The McCormick Reaper is a farming technology that was invented by
Cyrus H Cormick to harvest a ton of wheat, and only required one worker to use it. He patented
in 1834 and made a factory for it in 1847 and started producing his invention in Chicago, and by
1860, more than 100,000 reapers had been sold. (dude reaper is such a cool ass name for a
farming device) The important thing to note for this term here is that it’s just a byproduct of the
demand from farmers for better agricultural technology which in turn was caused by agriculture

Importance of Religion in RUral COmmunities 290 Religion brought farm communities together

Decline of the Tobacco Economy 294 Tobacco was a staple crop that made money, but the thing
was that the Tobacco Market was unstable as heck. Tobacco could be worth a lot of money one
day, and be worthless the next. In addition Tobacco drained the land of nutrients, making long
term Tobacco Farming almost impossible. This caused many to search for a new crop that
wouldn’t drain the land and also be more stable.
Some States Like Georgia and Florida tried growing rice. However rice required lots of irrigation
and had a long growing season of 9 months. Sugar was too labor intensive, and only the rich
could afford to grow it.
Short Staple Cotton 294 The South found their new cash crop in Short Staple Cotton. Short
Staple Cotton was a hardier and coarser strain of cotton that survived in a lot of differing
climates and soils. It also had harder to pluck seeds, but the cotton gin made that problem
irrelevant. So everyone started growing this cheaper, hardier, (less soft :( ) and overall better
profit cotton.
Spread of Cotton Production 295 Of course since this cotton was easier to grow and cheaper,
everyone started to grow Short Staple Cotton. By the 1850s, Cotton became the main thing
sustaining the Southern Economies, producing 3 million Bales a year in 1850.The Cotton price
fluctuated from overproduction, but even still, the Cotton Economy grew and grew. By the Civil
War, Cotton was ⅔ of All US exports.

[PAGE 92]
Expansion of Slavery 295 Because Cotton was such a valuable crop, the amount of slaves in
the South increased to meet the demand and the need for labor. The amount of slaves in places
like Alabama increased tenfold from 41,000 to 435,000
Weak Manufacturing Sector 295 Despite all the Agricultural growth, other forms of economic
activity in the South were lacking. Manufacturing and Merchants were miniscule compared to
the value of raw cotton in the South. However, the brokers that helped sell cotton or the “factors”
were still important, as they connected farmers to buyers of cotton. They often also served as
bankers, providing farmers with credit. Many in the south were in debt, especially during
economic drops in cotton prices.
Inadequate Regional Transportation System 297 The South’s banks were extremely
inadequate, and credit seemed to be nonexistent, resulting in most planters being in constant
debt due to drops in the cotton market. Thus, planters became super reliant on the merchants
that bought their product. In addition, The South also had terrible transportation infrastructure, to
the point where even the advanced canal and road systems seen in the North couldn’t influence
the growth in the South. The only hope was a railroad system that was built in the South, but
was so sparse that it held no significant change to transportation in the South. w
De Bow’s Review 298 The South soon realized just how dependent they were economically on
the North. They bought all their products from the North and sold all their products to the North.
(mainly because, as said before, terrible infrastructure and also the fact that the South made no
efforts to open up any ports). De Bow’s Review was an article written by James De Bow which
discussed the idea of an economically-independent South, stating how the fees they paid to the
North was a resulting loss of “$100,000,000” (maybe a slight exaggeration). Ironically enough,
the article was also mainly produced in the North, since New Orleans had no printing press, and
had advertisements in the article from nothing but Northern manufacturing firms. This only went
to show just how economically dependent the South was on the North.

owners to organize slaves to work. The Task system was one in which slaves were assigned
different tasks to do AMONG US , and the other more common system was the Gang system.
The Gang system was a system in which enslaved people were split into groups and were put
to work.

House Slaves 305 Household servants had a bit of an easier life than those who tended to the
fields. Some slaves served as domestic workers; cooking, cleaning, and nursing children and
stuff like that. These people lived closer to their masters and often gotta eat the leftovers of the
family dinners. Very few slaves developed almost familial relationships with their masters, but
the Majority of slaves resented their isolation and their lack of privacy. They often were punished
much more severely due to their proximity to their owners.
Sexual Abuse 305 Enslaved females that would work in the houses of plantation owners were
often subject to unconsenting sexual relationships with the owner. This caused enslaved
females that had these non-consensual relationships to be often harshly treated by the
plantation owner's wife out of basically jealousy. This included just being physically abused out
of nowhere, increased workloads, and other various, as the book puts it, “psychological
torment”(s).
Autonomy of Urban Slaves 306 Enslaved people in the Northern cities were treated a lot
differently than their counterparts in the south. For one, enslaved people in the Northern cities
often were sent out in the city to do tasks by their masters, meaning they got a lot more time to
mingle with free African Americans and White people, which resulted in many enslaved people
seeing much reason to free themselves or request their freedom. In addition to this factor, slave
owners in the North started to see less of a reason to have an enslaved person in their home
when they could instead choose to hire a heavily underpaid Irish immigrant to work for them as
well, resulting in an increasing urge for slave owners to ditch slavery as a whole, adding fuel to
the fire that was the abolitionist movement in the North. In the South, if Slaveowners wanted to
get any work done in the city or the North, they would often send an enslaved person to that
location to do the work for them, after all, city work is not as ‘refined’ or ‘cavalier-like’ as farm
work. They would also hire enslaved people to do the work for them, often in fields such as
mining, lumbering, or in the case of female enslaved people, textile work. It is still important to
note that even if the abolitionist movement was in full swing in the North, the abolitionist
movement only supported the removal of Slavery as a system, not the unjust treatment of
African Americans. In fact, many abolitionists had the motive to still keep African Americans and
White Americans separated, and many abolitionists still had racist ideas.

[PAGE 97]
TIghtened Restrictions on Free Blacks 307 Due to the rebellions such as Nat Turner’s rebellion,
many white southerners feared rebellions from both slaves and free African Americans.
Because of this fear, laws were created to limit the rights of Slaves even more, to the point
where legally it was pretty much impossible to free a slave. In addition, other laws restricted
travel HEAVILY for Slaves and Freed African Americans alike.
Slave Markets 307 The Slave Trade contributed to the dehumanization of the Enslaved people
in America. Slave traders were often given upwards of $1.7k(big money in today’s terms) for
trips, and at a minimum 500$, and were mostly asked to transport enslaved domestically from
one end of the South to another. To transport enslaved peoples, inhuman methods such as
coffles were used to keep groups of Enslaved people together, and on top of that, families were
often separated, children from parents, and parents from each other.
The Foreign Slave Trade 308 Ok so at this point, it is ILLEGAL to import slaves into the US.
However, slaves still are smuggled into the US (albeit not a lot) because people still want more
slaves. This led to a weird legal grey area for the US, as there WERE slave rebellions on many
of these illegal slave trading ships. The slaves that revolted were granted their freedom because
they weren’t considered legal slaves due to being imported.
Prosser and Turner Rebellions 309 In 1800, Gabriel Prosser gathered 1,000 rebellious slaves
outside Richmond; but two Africans gave the plot away, and the Virginia militia stopped the
uprising before it began. Prosser and 35 others were executed. In 1822, Denmark Vesey and
his followers made preparations for revolt, but the plan was leaked out again, and suppression
and retribution followed. In 1831, a man named Nat Turner led a band of African Americans who
armed themselves with guns and axes and killed 60 white men, women, and children before
being overpowered by state and federal troops.
Slave Resistance 310 Some slaves stole from their masters or from neighboring whites. Some
performed isolated acts of sabotage; losing or breaking tools (Southern planters gradually
began to buy unusually heavy hoes because so many of the lighter ones got broken) or
performing tasks improperly. Many African Americans resisted by building subtle methods of
rebellion into their normal patterns of behavior.
Pidgin 310 Pidgin is the name of a common language created by slaves that combines many
words from African cultures, and also bits of English as well. This language was created as
many different Slaves from different African nations had to communicate with each other.
Eventually Pidgin and English would combine into almost an accent or a way of speech for
generations.
Importance of Slave Spirituals 310 Music was especially important in slave society. In some
ways, it was as important to African Americans as language. Most important were voices and

[PAGE 98]
song; field workers often used songs to pass time in the fields. Since they sang them in the
presence of whites, they usually attached relatively innocuous words to them.

National Cultural Aspirations 315 America wanted to assert itself as a hub of both artistic and

Hudson River School 316 A “School” of American painters. They painted the vistas of the
rugged and still largely unsettled Hudson Valley. They considered nature the best source of
wisdom and spiritual fulfillment.
Cooper and the American Wilderness 316 James Fenimore Cooper. He wrote more than thirty
novels in the span of three decades, and was known to his contemporaries as a master of
adventure and suspense. What set him apart was his evocation of the American wilderness.
Cooper grew up in central New York, at a time when the edge of white settlement was not far
away.
Herman Melville 317 The new literary concern with the unleashing of human emotions did not

Southern Romanticism 317 Southern novelists of the 1830s (among them Beverly Tucker,

Ralph Waldo Emerson 318 Hey I know this guy! - a renowned essayist, activist, and abolitionist,
Ralph Waldo Emerson would start the Transcendentalist Movement, a movement focused on
the inherent goodness of people and nature. He would write many essays about Humanity and
their relationship to nature, and he would be considered an important intellectual in his era.
Thoreau and Civil Disobedience 318 Thoreau’s rejection of what he considered the artificial
constraints of society extended as well to his relationship with the government. In 1846, he went
to jail rather than agree to pay a toll tax. He insisted that he would not give financial support to a
government that permitted the existence of slavery.
Brook Farm 318 Although transcendentalism was above all an individualistic philosophy, it
helped spawn the most famous of all 19th century experiments in communal living: Brook Farm.
Brook Farm was an experimental community established by George Ripley in West Roxbury,
Massachusetts, in 1841.
There, according to Ripley, individuals would gather to create a new form of social organization.
All residents would share equally in the labor of the community so that all could share in the
leisure. In the end, the experiment was dissolved when tensions between the ideal of individual
freedom and the demands of a communal society rose to their highest, as well as when a fire
destroyed the central building of the community.
New Harmony 319 The failure of Brook Farm did not prevent the formation of other
experimental communities. Some followed in Ripley’s footsteps and borrowed from the ideas of
the French philosopher Charles Fourier, while others drew from the ideas of the Scottish
industrialist and philanthropist Robert Owen. Owen himself founded an experimental community
in Indiana in 1825, which he named New Harmony. It was to be a “Village of Cooperation,” in
which every resident worked and lived in total equality. The community was an economic failure,
but the vision that had inspired it continued to enchant Americans.

the Oneida Community, established in 1848 in New York by John Humphrey Noyes. The Oneida

family and marriage. Basically it was Polygamy but the creator lived in denial. Yeah, pretty much

The Shakers 320 The most distinctive feature of Shakerism was its commitment to complete
celibacy–which meant that no one could be born into the religion. Instead, all Shakers had to
choose it voluntarily. Shaker communities attracted about six thousand members in the 1840s,
more women than men; and members lived in conditions in which the contact between men and
women were very limited. Shakers openly endorsed the idea of sexual equality, and they even
embraced the idea of a God who was not clearly male or female.

[PAGE 100]
Women held the most power in the Shaker society. Shakerism was a refuge from the
“perversions of marriage” and “the gross abuses which drag it down.”
Joseph Smith 320 Leader of the Mormons, he published the Book of Mormon. According to
Smith, it was a translation of a set of golden tablets he had found in the hills of New York,
revealed to him by an angel of God. The Book of Mormon told the story of an ancient and
successful civilization in America, populated by one of the lost tribes of Israel who had found
their way to the new World centuries before Columbus.

Finney’s Doctrine of Personal Regeneration 322 Finney (The most influential revivalist leader of
the 1820s and 1830s) had the most success in Rochester, New York, where he had particular
success in mobilizing women. Finney discovered that it was effective to target the women in part
because of how appealing the message of revivalism was to them and because they provided
him with access to their male relatives.
American Society for the Promotion of Temperance 323 Although advocates of temperance had
been active since the late 18th century, the new reformers gave the movement an energy and
influence it had never previously known. In 1826, the American Society for the Promotion of
Temperance emerged as a coordinating agency among various groups.It attempted to use
many of the techniques of revivalism in preaching abstinence.
Cultural Divisions over Alcohol 323 The search for social discipline was particularly clear in the
battle over prohibition laws, which pitted established Protestants against new Catholic
immigrants, to many of whom drinking was an important social ritual and an integral part of the
life of their communities.

Rapid Growth of Public Education 325 - People started taking education more seriously, and
there were efforts to create a universal education system. In the 1830s, the belief that people
could achieve more with education led to many people trying to reform education. People like
Horace Mann of Massachusetts tried to reorganize the education system and get teachers to be
paid more. Other States did similar reforms, such as Pennsylvania passing a law that approved
State Funding for schools.

[PAGE 102]
By the 1850s, Tax funded schools were an accepted part of life in many states, and education
was starting to get reformed.
Some schools still sucked though, especially in rural areas like the west.
Achievements of Educational Reform 326 - Despite limitations and inequities, the achievements
of the school reformers were impressive by any standard. By the beginning of the Civil War, the
United States had one of the highest literacy rates of any nation.
The Benevolent Empire 326 - A similar emphasis on the potential of the individual sparked the
creation of new institutions to help the handicapped, institutions that formed part of a great
network of charitable activities known as the Benevolent Empire.
The Asylum Movement 326 - Asylums are created for criminals and for the mentally ill. On one
hand, in advocating prison and hospital reform, Americans were reacting to one of society’s
most glaring ills. Criminals of all kinds, debtors unable to pay their debts, the mentally ill, even
senile poor people–were crowded together into prisons and jails, which, in some cases, were
holes in the ground.
Prison Reform 326 - The creation of asylums for social deviants was not simply an effort to curb
the abuses of the old system. It was also an attempt to reform and rehabilitate the inmates. New
forms of rigid prison discipline were designed to rid criminals of the “laxness” that had
presumably led them astray.
Reform Movements and the Rise of Feminism 327 - Women in the 1830s and 1840s faced not
only all the traditional restrictions imposed on members of their sex by society, but also a new
set of barriers that had emerged from the doctrine of “separate spheres” and the transformation
of the family.
Seneca Falls Convention 330 - An organized meeting In 1848, where many people came to
discuss the very radical and crazy idea of Women’s rights. The meeting resulted in a
“Declaration of Sentiments” similar to the Declaration of Independence that stated that Women
deserve the same rights as men, including the right to vote. They wouldn’t get the right until
1920, but this is considered to be one of the main landmarks in the Push for Women’s rights.

Oregon Trail 344
The Oregon Trail was the major route westward which was 200miles long from the Great Plains
to past the Rocky Mountains. The trail took around 5 months(may to nov.) and many people
would die on this route. It was especially dangerous due to the snowy rockies.
Life on the Trail 346 Life on the Oregon Trail greatly affected the culture at which American
families separated roles. Men of the families were put to drive the wagons during the day,
whereas women were assigned the task of all the other chores such as cooking and laundry.
Indigenous peoples along the trail played a huge part in the success of many of the journeys to
the West, with many White families opting to trade with them in order to maintain momentum on
the trail. In addition, there is also a running idea that the many families that partook in this
journey were completely on their own, whereas in reality, many families actually traveled in
larger groups with other friends that decided they too, wanted to join on the journey.

that some old dying John C Calhoun was very in favor of ideas that supported Southern
independence on laws regarding slavery, such as telling the North to stop “attacking slavery”.

Taney’s Sweeping Opinion 359 A case known as Scott vs Sanford involved a former enslaved
person named Dred Scott who had recently gotten his freedom when his slave owner, a former
US army surgeon, had died. John Sanford, brother to the newly widowed wife of the surgeon,
argued that he had ownership over Scott, and the case was taken to the Supreme Court. Chief
Justice Roger Taney argued that African Americans had no real citizenship and therefore no
Constitutional rights, and were therefore property. Thus, by allowing Scott to be free, that was a
violation of property. This argument became super popular in the court, and unfortunately Scott
was not granted his freedom.
Why does this case matter? This case reinforced to people at the time the idea that enslaved
people were property, and reinforced ideas that enslaved people also deserved no
Constitutional rights. 😬

10,000. Still, this does not mean that the Lecompton Constitution was completely invalid, it only
told the government that the majority in Kansas did not like it. President Buchanan, a
