National Bank Acts 369 The National Bank Acts created a new interconnected Banking system
where Preexisting banks can join by investing a third of their total capital in the Government. In
return, these banks can issue US Treasury currency. This helped create a uniform system of
bank notes usable all over the North.
Financing the War 369 War is expensive, and the North tried many ways to fund it. They tried

money. Everything had a lot of drawbacks, people were against taxes, and the paper currency
caused a decent amount of inflation because the new money was not backed by gold. The main
way the North made money was through selling Bonds

turning point in the idea of Civil Rights in the nation of America. (Gettysburg Address)

The Impact of the North’s Victory 397 Of course, the North’s victory meant the destruction of the
Confederate Army and would lead to the Confederacy joining the Union of the United States,
but it also greatly strengthened the Northern economy, allowing for Industry and Railroad
development to boom, while the South’s planting economy was destroyed as the young male
population and the land was utterly gone. This brought the US in the direction of industry, as
now the economic growth of plantations would no longer be able to match that of the Industrial
economy in the North. However, far and away the most important victory (and perhaps the most
important victory in the entire US history, I would argue) was the freeing of the millions of
Africans Americans from bondage, and with Abraham Lincoln’s Emancipation Proclamation
bringing officially supported Civil Rights to the US(as in, public support from the President in
favor of the basic human rights of African Americans in the US), the US now headed into a new
age.

Freedom for the Ex-Slaves 401 For African Americans, the idea of freedom came in many
different forms. Pretty much everyone believed that they deserved the same rights as other
citizens, but the way they got those rights was a debated topic. Some wanted land as restitution,
while others simply wanted legal equality. Others wanted more opportunities for work. As soon
as emancipation occurred, many former slaves created their own communities and worked
together to rebuild.
The Freedmen’s Bureau 402 A government institution that kept troops in the south to ensure the
abolition of slavery, and also to help support the Millions of former slaves with things like getting
jobs and education. The Freedman’s Bureau was only a thing for a year, and it was too small
and underfunded to really fix any problems.
Conservative and Radical Republicans 403 In the Republican Party, there are divisions on the
way the Country is gonna do the whole Reconstruction thing. Conservatives just wanted the
SOuth to abolish slavery, while the Radicals such as Charles Sumner wanted that the South be
actively punished for their rebellion. They suggested that the South’s voting power should be
limited and that the government should put efforts in to enforce racial equality. Why is this so
radical? Idk man.
Lincoln’s 10% plan 403 Lincoln was more conservative, as his goal was to get the Country back
together again ASAP. Lincoln suggested that as long as Slavery is abolished and 10% of voters
promise to be good citizens again, the South can rejoin.

PROMISE to be good is going to do nothing.
Wade Davis Bill 403 Radical Republicans saw Lincoln’s plan and thought it was lame as hell, so
they created the Wade Davis Bill, which authorized the president to appoint governors for
conquered states. When each conquered state had a majority of the people pledge their
allegiance, they could put a convention together to elect people who didn’t participate on the
confederate side.
The Bill also wanted these new state governments to abolish slavery, remove voting rights for
high ranking Confederates and stuff like that.

Three Reconstruction Bills 406 Congress was able to establish 3 bills that would plan out
Reconstitution. Every Former Confederate State (minus Tennessee since they joined already)

out who could vote (all men who didn’t fight as a confederate) and then have them vote on a
new constitution and stuff. States would also have to ratify the 14th (and 15th for Texas and
Virginia since they were so slow to join) Amendment.
Fifteenth Amendment 406 This amendment forbade states to deny suffrage to citizens on
account of Race, Color, or Previous Condition of Servitude.

Protect Edwin M Stanton who was allied with the Radical Republicans.) The other law

Changing Gender Roles 412 As reconstruction was hard on just about everybody, especially
newly freed African American families, there was a large demand for just about any able bodied
person to start working. In particular, women, and specifically African American women started
to enter the workforce due to this demand and as such moved away from the more
home-centric role they once played in families.
Ulysses S. Grant 412 After seeing the demographics of republican and democratic voters in the

Panic of 1873 413 - After Jay Cooke and Company invested way too much into the railroad
infrastructure, it failed, and thus - banks became an untrustworthy source which then led to a
domino effect of banks collapsing all around! Debtors wanted the gov to redeem war bonds with
“greenbacks” - currency printed during the Civil War to increase money circulation. However,
Grant wanted a currency based on gold (and greenback was $356 mil in circulation). He tried
crushing the greenback dollar by passing the Specie Resumption Act - redeeming greenback
dollar for gold. This satisfied creditors but worried debtors because gold-based money could not
easily expand.
National Greenback Party 413 - “Greenbackers” formed their own political organization. Active
in the next 3 presidential elections. Kept the money issue alive but it failed to gain support.
Seward’s Folly 413 - William H. Seward fuckin hates President Johnson and decides to accept
Russian’s offer to sell Alaska for $7.2 mil. Lots of Americans didn’t like Alaska cuz it was just
cold with some random bears, so they called it “Seward’s Folly”.

Ku Klux Klan 414 oh no.
A Secret Society that worked to enforce a Democrat Majority in the south that also worked to
disenfranchise African Americans. The KKK would be formed in 1866 be led by former
Confederate Nathan Bedford Forrest. It quickly absorbed many smaller white supremacy groups
and became a big elaborate organization. The Klan ran many “Midnight rides” of men in white
masks and sheets to terrorize African American Communities.
The overall public perception of the KKK was positive by many White Southerners, who saw
them as a patriotic group.

overpower state courts and prosecute people who didn’t follow with the no discriminating thing.

Impact of Social Darwinism 415 If you remember from AP Modern World History, Social
Darwinism was the idea that unemployed or unsuccessful simply had a dandori issue and were

Hayes vs Tilden 415 Following the unsuccessful Ulysses S. Grant presidency, the Republican
party decided to choose Rutherford B. Hayes, as their candidate, while the Democratic party
decided on Samual J. Tilden. Both were committed to more moderate reforms, leaning more in
the conservative direction.
Tilden had a nearly 300,000 popular vote lead, but in terms of electoral votes, Tilden had 184
votes which was actually one short of majority. It all came down to Louisiana, South Carolina,
Florida, and Oregon, who combined had 20 disputed votes(basically undetermined votes).
Special Electoral Commission 415 Congress stepped in to try and decide for the 20 disputed
votes(See Hayes Vs. Tilden), and what they proposed and ended up doing was a meeting in
which 5 senators, 5 reps, and 5 supreme court justices from both political party would show up
to dispute the result of the election with 1 independent(undecided) justice, who was really a
pro-Republican and as such, Hayes was inaugurated into the position of US President on March
4th.
Compromise of 1877 416 Basically, during this whole Hayes vs Tilden thing politicians from both
sides of the Country were meeting to try and figure out how they would swiftly end the debate
on the election basically as soon as the political deadlock began. What ended up happening
was Democratic politicians agreed for the election of Hayes as long as the Republican party
promised to get American troops out of the South. This is ironic because Hayes was already
planning to withdraw the troops in the first place. Along with this one, there were some other
smaller requests such as the requirement of one Southerner in Hayes’s cabinet, “general

Ideological Limits 417 As the next 100 years stretch on, we will see the issue of racial injustice
being the forefront of the US, even after the abolishment of slavery. This is in part due to the
massive amount of adversity that those who supported the idea of a racially equal US would
have to face. The ideals of racism had been deeply ingrained in the US, and most people in the
country wouldn’t give a second to think about giving a chance to fixing racial injustice in the US.
Bourbon Rule 418 Hayes’ withdrawal of the military left many Southerners excited about the
return of their “Home Rule”, which was far from reality. In reality, the South was once again
under the rule of a powerful conservative oligarchy known as the “Bourbons” (or Redeemers.
This oligarchy was mostly merchants and people who got rich off of the railroads, and were
often corrupt and were very keen on concentrating power and money into their small circle. As
such, Reconstruction was greatly slowed down. Tax money began to flow less and less towards
infrastructure, and schools were even considered “not a necessity”.
THe Readjuster Challenge 418 A lot of people were able to notice this corruption in power of the
Southern upper class, and one of these groups were known as the “Readjusters”, who in
Virginia began their, well, “readjustment”, gaining a large following of mostly lower income White
folk and a fair number of African Americans. Their main argument was that the state had been
using debt payments in the wrong areas, and that they should instead opt to have the money go
into state services. Unfortunately, this movement was quickly put down using the power of racial
prejudice. Textbook says nothing more.
Henry Grady 419 Some Southerners concluded that the South needed Industrialization, and
that because the Southern economy had been so weak due to the lack of Industrialization, the
South was unable to win the Civil War. These people supported this idea of a “New South”, one
that would “out-Yankee the Yankees”. Henry Grady, an editor for the Atlanta Constitution was
one of these. The textbook says nothing about Henry Grady himself besides this, but I think it is
important to note that this ideal was not very popular with the vast majority of the South. Many
Southerners looked fervently in the direction of the Old South/Antebellum period and

Myth and Reality 431 As more people settled the West, the region became more familiar, turning
from the “Great American desert” into the “frontier” that we know so well. The lands were
considered empty and just waiting for white Americans to settle. This is not the reality, as we
know well enough by now. The American West had already been inhabited by people of all sorts
of cultures and backgrounds, ranging from Indigenous peoples all the way to Asian Americans.
These diverse cultures and backgrounds resulted in the wide range of diversity in the American
frontier.
Caste System 432 Holy shit India reference. Here’s a throwback to the start of the year:
Pueblos. The Pueblo people located in the Southwest region used an elaborate Caste system in
their society. If you don’t know what a caste system is, a caste system is basically a social
hierarchy/pyramid. Thanks to the Spanish influence, the Pueblo people had a caste system
based on, you guessed it, race, with Spanish and Mexicans at the top and Indigenous peoples
who were either no longer part of their respective tribe or PoWs, or in a lot of cases, enslaved at
the bottom.
Plains Indians 432 “Plains Indians” were groups of Native Americans that resided in the
American MidWest. Their tribes were in constant alliances and conflict with each other at all
times, had elaborate economies, differing languages, but overall had similar traits due to their
close contact. Most tribes were split into “bands” of 500 men and women combined, who would
mostly self govern. In these tribes, gender roles were very split, with men heading the military
and hunting, while women did the cooking and raised children. These groups were mostly
agricultural. Yeah I mean that’s it.
One important thing that is hard to fit anywhere is the importance of the Sioux people. The
Sioux people were considered the most powerful tribe, as their military prowess was unmatched
by the rest of their peers and was in large part due to their strong horses that were gained from
the Spanish generations ago.
Economic Importance of the Buffalo 432 Buffalo became a massive part of the lifestyle of the
Plains Indigenous people in America. The Skin was used for clothing and other cloth-related
stuff, flesh was used for meat, bones were used for tools, tendons for bowstrings, and even the
manure for fertilizer.
Indian Weaknesses 433 It was only a matter of time for the Plains Indigenous people before
they were eventually invaded by white Americans. Their land was already deemed American
territory and they still lacked immunity to many diseases, along with the technological and
economical advantage that White Americans had.
Taos Indian Rebellion 433 After the Americans invaded and acquired New Mexico, General
Stephen Kearny was put under the control of the region, and was assigned the responsibility of
assigning governors to the region. Ignoring the overwhelming majority of 50,000 Mexicans in the
region, Kearny opted to choose from the pool of 1,000 White Americans. This outraged the

[PAGE 130]
people of the New Mexico region, and in 1847, a group of Taos Indigenous peoples rebelled and
killed some of these chosen governors.

prevented Chinese laborers from entering the country, except for merchants, which is a loophole

had 10 year extensions for a while) in 1902.

Limited Social Mobility 439 Even though people could move to the west, people couldn’t really
improve their social situation. The poor remained relatively poor, and the rich people had the

[PAGE 132]
opportunity to buy a bunch of new land and become richer. In the end, wealth distribution had
become relatively similar to the Eastern parts of America.
Racially Stratified WOrking Class 439 The working class was a diverse group of people of many
races. Too bad everyone hated each other. White workers typically were able to climb socially
and economically, while everyone else was condemned to manual labor for most of their lives.
In addition, WHITE employers typically assumed that Chinese, Mexican, Filipino, and other not
white workers were better for manual labor, and therefore yearned for the mines.
Life Cycle of a Mining Boom 439 After the 1848 California Gold Rush and it’s subsequent
smaller Gold Rushes, miners actually found great fortune mining out the mountains and
plateaus of the region which were rich in all sorts of minerals. This mining boom lasted for
around 30 years before it eventually collapsed (these resources after all are limited, so as time
goes on, profits diminish). This was in fact the case for all the Gold and Silver rushes that went
on in California, with miners moving from one mineral to another, first Gold and Silver, then Lode
and Quartz, and then whatever was leftover, until the land was devoid of all precious metals.
Comstock Lode 439 During the period of the Colorado Gold Rush of 1859(another large and
famous Gold Rush), miners found an abundance of Comstock Lode(named after Henry
Comstock) in the Nevada region, specifically in the Washoe district. This led to a ton of
Californians moving in search of this Lode fortune in Nevada, and as a result led to the
development and settlement of the mostly White settler-free Nevada.

Drought 454 In the plains states, water was mostly obtained from the Mississippi river, and
when your source for water is limited to just 1, it becomes REALLY hard to get consistent water
at all times. In 1887, a long dry season began, turning fertile lands into deserts. Farmers turned
to “dryland farming”, where farmers would use deep wells powered by windmills to get water
from underground. Unfortunately, this was not enough to help out farmers, and government
intervention or funding was desperately needed.
Hard Times for Farmers 454 Due to the droughts in the 1880s, farmers obviously found it pretty
hard to be alive. Crop prices also fell to the floor, and because many of them had debts to pay, a
lot of farmers started moving back East towards their old homes and families. This resulted in
many ghost towns across the plains area that can probably still be found today.
Consequences of Overproduction 455 Overproduction of specific crops was happening globally.
This caused the American farming industry to crash. I mean, this is nothing new, the past 2
terms have been saying this I don’t know. This resulted in many poor farmers who could not
afford land. So who profits the most off of this? Landlords that rent out land to the farmers.
Isolation 455 Because so many people were leaving for the East, farm families that stayed West

Moving Assembly Line 463 In 1914, Henry Ford introduced the technique of the assembly line,
cutting the time for assembling parts of a car from 12 to 1 hour, and slashed prices of the Model
T down to a third of the price because of the sheer amount of cars that could be assembled.
Rapid Expansion of the Railroad 463 During the 19th century, the largest growing US
infrastructure project was far and away the railroad. From 1860 to 1900, around 140,000 miles
of rail were constructed. To fund the construction of the railroads, investors were needed, and
those that invested in these railroads the most became some of the most powerful people in the
country, such as Cornelius Vanderbilt, James J Hill and Collis P Huntington.
Limited Liability 464 During the 1830s and 1840s, companies started to sell stock to the public
to raise money and incentivise investors. This practice only grew as America became more and
more corporate. Affluent members of American society began putting large portions of their
money into said stock and this was viewed as investments. The appeal of these investments
was that by buying stock, they only had “limited liability”, meaning that even if they lost money,
they wouldn’t lose that much, and that they as investors were not very liable if the company
failed.
Andrew Cargenie 464 A Scottish immigrant who opened a Pittsburgh steel work in 1873,
Andrew Carnegie became one of the largest figures in the industry using a wide range of
techniques to dominate the steelworks industry in the US. Carnegie became by far the most

[PAGE 138]
successful and powerful figure in the steel and railroad market, amassing a fortune so great in
stock that he eventually cashed out to JP Morgan for $450,000,000, an absurd amount in

New Managerial Techniques 465 New management techniques were also found in the US, with
Gustavus Swift developing the “middle manager” executive, which would form a layer of
command between workers and owners (basically a company hierarchy). These techniques
punctured every hole in the US corporate world, and are the basis for American corporate
culture today and across the globe honestly (hence why East Asian corporate life is so fucking
miserable).
Horizontal and Vertical Integration 465 Big companies gained more profit through 2 means:
horizontal integration and vertical integration. Horizontal integration was basically combining a
bunch of companies into the same enterprise, and vertical integration was the method in which
companies would take over other businesses that said company relied on, for example a tech
company acquiring the company that manufactures its products.
Rockefeller’s Standard Oil 465 John Rockefeller’s Standard Oil was a prime example of using
Horizontal and Vertical integration to its fullest potential, becoming one of the largest corporate
empires in the US, and effectively one of the first monopolies, controlling 90% of the refined oil
in the US.
The Trust Agreement 465 The “trust”, pioneered by Standard Oil and perfected by JP Morgan,
the “trust” as an agreement in which stockholders in smaller companies transferred their stock
to a group of investors, and then the original stockholders would then buy shares of those
investors they just gave their stock to.
Rapid Corporate Consolidation 467 America had become a country of monopolies. Under 1% of
companies owned 33% of the manufacturing market, and bankers such as JP Morgan and
industrial figures such as Rockefeller had an incredible amount of power over the American
population and economy.

practice it was nearly impossible. The American corporate ladder was a thin, creaky one that
only a select few could ever hope to climb (unless you were born rich).

Rapid Urban Growth 487 By the 1920s, a majority of the AMerican people lived in urban areas,
anywhere with 2500 people ore more. New York had almost 3 million people, and other cities
had similar levels of growth. The city attracted many people from rural areas with conveniences,
entertainment, and a new way to experience American culture not found in small rural towns.
Geographic Mobility 488 As cities became more urban, many more people moved out of Rural
areas to live in cities. A decent amount of people moving were young women who had little
opportunity in rural farms.
African American Communities 489 Chat we are back at it again!! By the end of the 19th
century, there were substantial African American communities in over thirty cities, many of them
in the South. Black communities established in the late 19th century paved the way for the great
population movements of the future.
THe Diverse American City 490 Other countries that were experiencing heavy immigration in
this period were facing immigrants coming from one or two sources. For example, in Argentina,
almost everyone was coming from Italy and Spain. However, in the United States, substantial
groups arrived from all over the world, and, in some towns, a dozen different ethnic groups
found themselves living in close proximity.
Benefits of Ethnic Communities 491 Immigrant neighborhoods offered newcomers familiarity in
a foreign country. They could find newspapers and theaters in their native languages, sotres
selling their native foods, and more. Many immigrants also maintained close ties with their
native countries, and some immigrants helped bring the rest of their families to America.
Americanization 491 Many immigrants came to America with romantic visions of the New World,
Changing Gender Roles 492 Due to the urge for immigrants to assimilate into American life,

or had mothers that were a lot more subordinate to their husbands would be forced to have the
females in the family also go off and work for money.
Nativism 492 Some Americans thought of the newly immigrated Europeans as “Europe’s human
and inhuman rubbish”, and this idea was also carried to the West coast in prejudice against
Mexican, Chinese, and Japanese immigrants. This is the idea behind Nativism. Such an idea is

[PAGE 143]

Immigration Restriction League 492 In 1887, Henry Bowers, a lawyer well known for his hatred
against Catholics and foreigners founded the American Protective Association, which, as you
guessed, was committed to stopping immigration to the US. The fact that this group reached a
staggering 500,000 members went to show just how widespread Nativism was.
Later, a group of 5 Hardvard Alumni started the Immigration Restriction League, which was not
as terrifying as Henry Bower, but instead concluded that immigrants should be given a literacy
test to determine whether or not they were to be allowed into the States.
Advantages of Cheap Labor 494 Cheap labor was what led the American economical machine
to keep running during this time period, and the financial incentive drowned out the arguments
made for literacy requirements, leading to President Grover Cleveland to veto the usage of said
literacy requirements.
Fredred Law Olmstead and Calvert Vaux 494 Around the mid 19th century, cities had started to
pop up, and would feature a lot more sophisticated planning from architects and planners and
the like. Frederick Law Olmstead and Calvert Vaux, two landscape designers, were the driving
force behind New York’s Central Park. This turned out to be a huge success, and the 2 were
called up to work on other parks in cities such as Brooklyn, Boston, Philly, Chicago, and DC.
City Beautiful Movement 494 Cities now had a large motivation to attempt to make themselves

Growth of SUburbs 495 The land on the edges and outskirts of cities became quite cheap for
the average American middle class person, and as such the American suburbs as we know
them started to grow. In the 1870s, Chicago hosted nearly 100 suburban areas connected to the
city.
Tenements 495 For the average person in the US however, it was pretty unreasonable to even

Transportation Problems 496 With that many people in one small area, a large problem in the

Air Pollution 498 The Industrial Revolution led to a lot of decay in the environment. The obvious
problems like fires, disease, and crowding were seen as a result of industrialization. Pollution
was also a major issue due to the improper disposal of waste both human and industrial. At the
time, the common practice was to dump all waste into the rivers, which also happened to be
where EVERYONE GOT THEIR WATER FROM.
Air Quality sucked due to the burning of coal from factories, and furnaces from almost every
building in a city. This led to a massive amount of respiratory problems, which led to calls for
reform.
Public Health Service 498 The Public Health Service was a government established group that
was tasked with finding the causes of and preventing occupational diseases. It attempted to set
health standards, but lacked the power to enforce them. The Public Health Service was an
important albeit small step in the government recognizing the importance of public health and
labor safety.

High Crime Rates 499 Poverty and crowded cities led to lots of crime. Most of the crime was

Boss Rule 499 Boss Rule, or Political Machines were an important institution created during the
Gilded Age. Many immigrants needed support adjusting to a new American Urban Lifestyle, and
needed assistance with jobs and finding homes and being alive and having food and stuff.
Politicians turned to “boss rule”, where people would pledge their votes in a certain way in
exchange for relief in the form of food, coal, or potentially even jobs.
Graft and Corruption 500 Political machines also made a lot of money through corrupt means,
such as buying land where proposed developments were going to be made, or getting
kickbacks from contractors and construction companies. This led to Bosses getting RICH AS
HELL (and sent to jail).
Reasons for Boss Rule 500 Boss Rule seems pretty corrupt and stupid, but the reasons for
boss rule make sense. Many Immigrants who held voting power didn’t really care about morality
more than making a living. In addition, Bosses often helped people more than the government,
which made people feel loyal or indebted to bosses. Basically Bosses did stuff the government

Middle Class Culture 500 It was in the late 19th and early 20th centuries that the US middle

Rising income 500 Due to the increased financial output of the American industrial economy,

middle managers. However, even higher than that were the doctors, lawyers, and eventually
engineers (basically the jobs that our parents work).
New Merchandising Techniques 501 The American market grew and grew, and this was due to
the emergence of once highly skilled workers and hard to learn professions becoming jobs for
factory machines to do. Clothes for example, once had to be custom tailor-made, were now
being easily made by factories and sold cheaply to the world.

[PAGE 146]
Chain Stores 501 Local businesses became in danger of losing to nearby “chain stores” such as
A&P, a large grocery store chain in the US that started after the civil war. There was no reason
to support a local business besides personal connection and loyalty than to just go and look at
the wider variety and cheaper selection of goods that chain stores had.
Social COnsequences oF Mail ORder Catalogs 501 Chain stores failed to open new stores in
the rural and remote areas of the US, but managed to still reach their customers there in the
form of mail-ordering. Montgomery Ward would send a catalog of goods to these areas, offering
thousands of items for cheap prices, and this technique became very popular for other large
retail businesses like Sears and Roebuck.
Impact of the Department Store 501 Department Stores are widely responsible for the act of
shopping becoming a hobby in the US and honestly across the world. The large number of all
sorts of goods particularly in fashion, the salesclerks, and collection of luxury goods allowed the
American consumerism model to thrive.

Sherman Antitrust Act 518 What is an Antitrust? An antitrust is a way for the legislature to
prevent the spread of monopolies and to promote competition in the economy. The Sherman
Antitrust Act was smoothly passed in 1890, but over the decade it was enforced, it failed to
deliver, and antitrust suits were more commonly used against labor unions rather than
businesses that ruled over the US economy as monopolies. It was unclear and many court
rulings found loopholes.
McKinley Tariff 518 The McKinley Tariff was a tariff that put a 50% duty on foreign imports,
basically banning any foreign imports because of the absurdly high cost. Pretty simple here,
McKinley is attempting to increase domestic production and competition.
Interstate Commerce Act 519 Midwestern farmers (Grangers were a big part of this (see term
right below!)) persuaded state legislatures to pass acts that would regulate railroads in the
1870s Why? A lot of railroads in the US were monopolies that were getting out of hand.

[PAGE 149]
The Supreme Court later ruled that the state laws put in place against the railroads were
unconstitutional in the Wabash case,making it clear that railroads would be under regulation of
the federal government from now on.
After this ruling, Congress would succumb to public pressure with the Interstate Commerce Act,
which would basically bring the railroad monopolies down declaring that interstate rail rates had
to be “reasonable and just”.
Grangers (term name is “origins but it makes no sense in this context) 520 The Grange was the
first major farming organization in the 1860s. Started by Oliver H Kelley, Kelley abandoned his
job in the government and founded the Grange Patrons of Husbandry, attempting to bring
farmers together to develop new farming techniques and tech. Membership soared as farm
prices decreased in 1873, and by 1875 had nearly 800,000 members.
Farmers started this because during the 1880s, American farmers were suffering from economic
decline, and knew that the American economy was headed in a direction of industrialization.
Their protest became one of the most influential movements in the US, starting the Populist
party and American Populism as a whole.
Economic Grievances 521 With the Depression of 1873 prices of farms decreased causing an
increase in peoples economic intrest of agriculture. With this change, many of the midwest
lodges shifted their focus from social organization to farms.
Political Program 521 The Grangers worked to elect state legislators that would support their
ideals, sometimes even running their own candidates. In the 1870s, they started by trying to get
laws passed that would regulate railroad practices, and were quite successful in doing so. (look
at interstate commerce act, the rest of this is just a repeat of that)
Mary Lease 521 In 1880, the Grangers lost a large portion of their members, dropping to just
100,000 members. It’s successor was the Farmers Alliance started by Southern farmers, and
had nearly 4 million members by 1880.
A big difference here was the involvement of women. The Farmers Alliance was not afraid of
having women as full voting members or having women hold high positions. Mary Lease for
example, was a very prominent Populist orator, with one of her most famous quotes being to
“raise less corn and more hell.” which goes SO FUCKING HARD.
Birth of the People’s Party 522 Due to the influence of the Farmers Alliance and their growing
sentiment, 1300 delegates met in Omaha to create a new party, the People’s Party, otherwise
known as the Populists under the head of Leonidas L. Polk.
Although young, this party was no joke, and in 1892 their presidential candidate James B.
Weaver got nearly 1 million votes and 22 electoral votes. The party elected 3 governors, 5

[PAGE 150]
senators, and 10 congressmen, while also converting many Republicans and Democrats to their
cause.
Free Silver 523 The Populist party endorsed the idea of free silver, which would make silver a
basis of currency along with gold. This would allow for a cheaper currency to be a standard for
the US and therefore give better financial stability to those who could not afford gold.
Colored Alliances 523 Because of the similar situations of financial poverty that both White
populists and African Americans faced in the US, it became a lot easier for White populists to
accept African Americans as allies, therefore creating “colored alliances”. A majority of populists
were fine with this idea, albeit with the exception that African Americans could not have control
or high positions.
Populist Platform 523 Populists had a multitude of different ideas, one being the “subtreasury”
system in which the government would establish warehouses across the US that would work as
basically a crop bank. Using these warehouses, farmers could borrow money from the
government and sell their crops when prices were good, basically a way for farmers to ensure
maximum profit. Populists also wanted to get rid of national banks, and to improve the ability of
everyday people to influence the political environment of the US.

Populism’s Ideological Challenge 524 Populists emphatically rejected the Laissez-faire ideas of
their time, including the idea that the rights of ownership are absolute.
Overexpansion and Weak Demand 524 Another main cause of The Panic of 1893. Depressed
prices in agriculture decreased the buying power of the farmers, the largest population group.
Depression conditions in Europe caused a loss of American markets abroad and a withdrawal
by foreign investors of gold. (Woah supply and demand!!! :0)

advocating a massive public works program to create jobs for the unemployed and an inflation

[PAGE 151]
of the currency. When it was clear that his plan wasn’t being considered in congress, Coxey

“Crime of ‘73’” 526 WAS THAT THE CRIME OF 73????? In 1873, a law was passed that made
Silver money basically useless. Silver coins dropped to a 16th of their price. This put basically
any silver miner out of business, and the demonetization of silver became the “crime of 73”. This
scandal sent the US into problems when it came to currency for the following couple decades.
Symbolic Importance of The Currency Question 526 Silver becoming so cheap actually posed a
positive side, as it provided cheap currency for the common person to use, and as such, the
differing opinions of gold vs silver began to rise in the US in the form of symbolism. Using gold
was seen as rich and affluent, while silver was the people’s money and wasn’t corrupt like gold.

governor of Ohio. His biggest contribution to the US was the reinstatement of the Gold standard
and making Silver have value again which in turn also made it less unique in the economy since
it was no longer dirt cheap.

Jane Addams and Hull House 553 The most famous of these settlement houses was Hull
House, which opened in Chicago as a result of social worker Jane Addams. It became a model
for more than 400 similar institutions throughout the nation. These houses sought to help
immigrant families adapt to the language and customs of their new country.
These houses avoided the condescension and moral disapproval of earlier philanthropic efforts,
and embraced a belief that middle-class Americans had a responsibility to help others and teach
them how to create middle-class lifestyles.
Jane Addams started Hull House, a settlement house that helped poor immigrants adjust to
American life by providing education, childcare and social services. Became a model for
hundreds for 400 similar reform centers.
How did they differ from traditional charities? - Older charity groups often acted as though
“you’re poor cuz u made bad choices” but Hull House believed that poor immigrants just needed
support, that middle class people had a responsibility to help and help families to build stable
middle class lives.
American Medical Association 555 The AMA (but not of the reddit variety) was a national

doctors. State governments responded by requiring all doctors to receive a license from the
state. In addition to all this, the AMA led to the more proper education of those in the medical
field, with Medical schools (Like John Hopkinds in Baltimore) being compared favorably to the
top schools in Europe.

A Public Space for Women 558 The Women’s clubs served as places for Women to meet and

Women’s Trade Union League 559 Founded in 1903 by female union members and upper-class
reformers (No names??), it was committed to persuading women to join unions. The WTUL also

Radical Challenge of Women’s Suffrage 559 Back then, suffrage seemed to many of its critics a
very radical demand, and that rationale was used by some early supporters to advance it.
Throughout the late 19th century, many suffrage advocates presented their views in terms of
“natural rights,” arguing that women deserved the same rights as men, including the right to
vote.
Opponents said that suffrage was a threat to the “natural order” of civilization. Antisuffragists,
many of whom were women, associated suffrage with divorce(many suffrage advocates also
supported making it easier for women to obtain a divorce), with promiscuity, immorality, and
neglect of children.
NAWSA 559 Led by Anna Howard Shaw and Carrie Chapman Catt, membership in the National
American Woman Suffrage Association grew from 13,000 to over 2 million in 24 years. The

[PAGE 160]
movement gained strength because many of its most prominent leaders began to justify
suffrage in “safer,” less threatening ways.
Conservative Arguments for Suffrage 560 Some of the reasons for Women’s suffrage were not
too nice. For example, many people in the Middle Class believed that African Americans and
Immigrants could vote, then white women should have the right to vote too (so basically racism).
Nineteenth Amendment 560 In 1920, the 19th Amendment gave women the right to vote. Some
feminists didn’t accept the justification for the 19th Amendment and wanted a more explicit
constitutional amendment, but many thought this was too radical. This amendment also did not
give ALL American women the right to vote. mainly just white women. African American, Asian,

Reforming Government 560 These progressive reforms that were carried out by the American
people were all missing one thing though: government intervention. It was impossible for these
reforms to happen at a national level without said intervention, and it quickly became the goal of
these movements to get the government to provide support in the form of new laws and
regulation.
Middle-Class Progressives 561 New generations of middle class citizens were nothing like their
parents when it came to their ideas on activism. While their pops and ma believed their time
was better put someplace else than something as “vulgar” as working-class politics, the kids
were very comfortable with speaking out against the government and joining in on the activist
movements.
Commission Plan 561 One of the first major successes came in Galveston, Texas, where the
old city government proved unable to deal with the effects of a destructive tidal wave in 1900.
Capitalizing on public dismay, reformers, many of them local businessmen, won approval of a
new city charter. The mayor and council were replaced by an elected, nonpartisan commission.
Other cities soon followed.
City-Manager Plan 561 Besides the Commission Plan, the City-Manager Plan was another plan
used in American cities to replace the charter and mayor system. The City Manager Plan would
have a City Council decide on an outside influence and city planner to help run the city,
therefore an unbiased source to help manage the city.
Tom Johnson Some major reform leaders worked inside regular political systems after
progressives gained influence. Tom Johnson, a famous reform mayor from Cleveland fought
powerful streetcar companies to lower their fares to 3 cents and pushed for the city to own
important public utilities. After he died and lost power, his aide Newton D.Baker became mayor
and helped Cleveland earn a reputation as one of the best cities in the country. Other reform
mayors like Hazen Pingree of Detroit and Samuel “Golden Rule” Jones of Toledo also fought
corrupt party bosses and brought reform ideas into city govs.

[PAGE 161]
Initiative & Referendum Two major reforms first suggested by Populists in the 1890s were
Initiative and the Referendum.
No.1 - Initiative -> let citizens propose new laws and vote on them directly rather than going
through state lawmakers.
No.2 - Referendum -> Allowed voters to approve or reject laws that state legislatures have
already passed.
Direct Primary and Recall Reforms like direct primaries and the recall were meant to weaken
political party bosses and give more power to regular voters.
The primary election let voters choose candidates instead of party leaders choosing them
behind the scenes. In the South some white leaders supported primaries cuz they thought it
would be easier to limit black voters compared to normal elections.
On the other hand, the recall allowed voters to remove an elected official from office by holding
a special election as long as enough people signed a petition.
Robert La Follette One of the most famous reform leaders at the state level. Elected governor in
1900, he helped make Wisconsin a model for progressive reform. He brought in direct
primaries, initiatives and referendums, giving people more power in gov decisions. He regulated
railroads and public utilities, passed workplace safety laws and created compensation for
worker injuries. Taxes were increased on large inherited fortunes and taxes on railroad and big
corporations doubled. He was a strong leader who helped people understand progressiveness
and believed reform should involve the people, media, schools, organizations and politicians.
THIS GUY IS FIGHTING B.O.B (nickname)
Decline of Party Influence Progressive reforms weakened political parties, leading to less voter
turnout rate. In the late 1800s, strong party loyalty meant about 81% of voters participated in
elections. By 1900, that number dropped to 73% and by 1912 it fell to 59% never reaching 70%
ever again.
Why did this happen? - Secret ballots (where voters vote privately without anyone knowing their
choice) made it harder for party bosses to control voters, especially illiterate ones. But it was
mainly due to the rise of “interest groups” like labor unions, business associations, women’s
clubs and social reform groups which had gained influence outside of the party system.
Triangle Shirtwaist Fire In 1911, the Triangle Shirtwaist Factory fire in NYC killed 146 workers,
mostly women that were trapped by locked exits. A state commission studied the workplace
conditions and called for reforms. By 1914, lawmakers like Robert F.Wagner and Alfred E.Smith
passed strong labor laws regulating factories and enforcing safety rules that responded to public
pressure from unions & women’s groups.

[PAGE 162]
W.E.B. Du Bois Born free in Massachusetts and educated at Harvard and Fisk, W.E.B. Du Bois
opposed Booker T. Washington’s approach. In his book The Souls of Black Folk , he criticized
Washington’s desire for limiting black ambitions and accepting segregation. Du Bois argued that
true progress required full political and civil rights.
NAACP Founded Du Bois believed that African Americans should get university education and
be able to get professional careers and fight for civil rights. So in 1905 the guy started the
Niagara Movement and in 1909 it merged with white allies to form the NAACP with him as
leader. It fought for equality in court cases. Du Bois also campaigned against lynching, with the
assistance of Ida Wells Barnett & Jessie Daniel Ames.
WCTU Inspired by the temperance movement before the Civil War, the Women's Christian
Temperance Union was an organization led by Frances Willard that grew to 245k members by
1911. Along with the Anti-Saloon League, they pushed for laws banning saloons as well as the
prohibition of alcohol.
Eighteenth Amendment Support for prohibition grew despite opposition from immigrants and
workers. By 1916, 19 states had banned alcohol. WW1 and moral support from rural
fundamentalists helped pass a national ban. In 1919, the 18th Amendment was ratified, starting
national prohibition in 1920.
Eugenics and Nativism In the early 1900s, many Americans wanted limited immigration. Some
used eugenics, claiming certain races were “better” and that immigrants would spread
undesirable traits. Eugenicists ranked races and pushed forced sterilization for the “unfit,” like
criminals or people with disabilities. People like Madison Grant warned that immigrants from
Eastern Europe, Latin America, and Asia threatened the “purity” of white Americans.
Even people who didn't care about race supported the limits to reduce overcrowding and
unemployment in urban areas. Theodore Roosevelt liked this despite opposition from employers
and immigrant groups. By WW1, nativism grew stronger.
Eugene Debs From 1900-14, socialism gained support in the US more than almost any other
time. The Socialist party grew from <100k voters in 1900 to 1 mil for Eugene V. Debs (their
leader) in 1912. German and Jewish immigrants and some farmers in the south/midwest
supported the socialist party. Socialists won over 1k local & state offices, supported by thinkers
like Lincoln Steffens & Walter Lippmann. Women reformers like Florence Kelley & Frances
Willard liked socialism for its support of peace and workers’ rights.
Wobblies All socialists wanted big changes to the economy, but they disagreed on how much
change was needed and how to achieve it. Some wanted radical European-style Marxism while
others wanted smaller reforms, keeping some private businesses. Some tried to make change
through voting, while others supported strikes and protests.

[PAGE 163]
The IWW (Industrial Workers of the World, their nickname was the Wobblies), led by Big Bill
Haywood, was a radical union that wanted all workers in one union. They also wanted to end
unfair wages. They used strikes instead of politics and were sometimes linked to bombings.
They helped unskilled workers in the west like miners and timber workers. After a big timber
strike in 1917, the gov arrested the leaders and passed laws against them. The union survived
but never became strong again.
Socialism's Demise Most socialists were moderate and wanted slow, peaceful change through
politics. They tried to teach people why change was needed and work within the system. But
WW1 hurt them a lot cuz they didn’t support the war, and the gov and public began attacking
and harassing them.
The Problem of Corporate Centralization Louis Brandeis and his supporters didn’t like big
businesses cuz they were inefficient & unfair. They believed big businesses hurt people’s
freedom, gave too much power to too little people and caused abuse. He said the gov should
make rules to keep big companies from getting too strong.
“Good Trusts” and “Bad Trusts” Some progressives didn’t worry about big businesses as much
as Brandeis did. They cared more about efficiency and thought big companies could be helpful
if controlled. According to them, the gov’s job was to stop abuses and support “good trusts” and
punish “bad trusts”. Since big businesses weren’t going away, the gov needed to keep an eye
on them. Leaders like Herbert Croly and Walter Lippmann wanted better coordination in the
economy, either by businesses working together or by the gov regulating more. Theodore
Roosevelt later supported the idea, saying the gov should supervise and control big companies.
He became a major symbol for national reform.
Roosevelt’s Background Roosevelt kinda created himself a reputation of being a “wild man” cuz
of his energy and bold actions. In NY, he was an energetic lawmaker, in the Dakotas, he hunted
outlaws, as police commissioner, he fought crimes, as assistant secretary of the navy, he
supported US expansion and as a rough rider, he led his famous charge into Kettle Hill, Cuba.
Roosevelt’s Vision of Federal Power Roosevelt worked with progressives who wanted to control,
but not destroy, big businesses. His main goal was to give the government power to investigate
companies and share what they found with the public. The Department of Commerce and
Labor, created in 1903, helped with this through the Bureau of Corporations.
Northern Securities Company Roosevelt wasn’t really against big businesses, but he did take on
a few major trusts to show that the gov could actually do stuff. So in 1902, he used the Sherman
Antitrust Act to break up the Northern Securities Company, a huge railroad monopoly made by
JP Morgan. The Supreme Court agreed in 1904. Roosevelt then filed over 40+ antitrust cases
but didn’t try to stop big businesses from growing overall.
Roosevelt also treated labor disputes more fairly than previous presidents. In a 1902 coal strike,
he pushed both the miners and owners to accept federal arbitration (no clue wtf that is). When

[PAGE 164]
the owner refused, he threatened to seize the mines and the strike ended with higher wages
and shorter hours for the workers, though not with full union recognition. Roosevelt also
sometimes used troops to support employers in other strikes.
Hepburn Act 574 You remember the Interstate Commerce Act? The one that would regulate
railroads in the US? While it was in full effect, the full effect was barely noticeable and the
Railroad industry in the US still proved to be one of the most corrupt and monopoly-driven
businesses in the US. The Hepburn Act instituted early on by Teddy Roosevelt due to popular
demand from the public would strongly increase the power of the Interstate Commerce Act,
allowing for the Act which had already been in power for a while to actually do something.
Pure Food And Drug Act 574 Roosevelt also got the Pure Food and Drug Act enacted, which
would restrict the sale of dangerous medicines. Upton Sinclair’s The Jungle was one of the
largest catalysts for this, which described the horrible conditions of the meatpacking industry
(remember thumbs in sausages?).
Unrelated note: Roosevelt was an early supporter of better working conditions, arguing for an 8
hour workday, compensations for work-related injuries, and stock market regulation
Federal Aid To The West 575 Teddy Roosevelt was a supporter in the American conservation
movement, a movement which would preserve the US and it’s natural habitats. In order to aid
them, Roosevelt backed the Newlands Act, which would provide funding for the construction of
infrastructure, MOST IMPORTANTLY: irrigation projects in the undeveloped West. Nowadays,
we might think of construction as a means of destroying natural habitats if anything, but it
worked back then because by allowing control of the water, more water would be pumped into
the natural habitats of the US.
Competing Conservationist Visions 576 For over a decade, an argument raged between
naturalists and advocates of Hetch Hetchy Dam(Woah this sounds familiar!!). This battle
consumed the energies of our beloved John Muir, and many believe that it led to his death.
The dam was placed on the ballot in 1908, and San Franciscans approved it by a huge margin.
Tennessee Coal And Iron Company 576 got a fuckin long ass grind baby lets get to it
Don’t worry much about the actual term above: the real issue here is that despite Roosevelt’s
pretty progressive and positive changes, the US was still in quite the recession. Banks were
doing terrible and it was clear that there was action that needed to be taken. To combat this, JP
Morgan quickly threw up a bunch of crappy New York banks that work as temporary cover for
the recession. In order to get that money though, JP Morgan (more specifically his company, US
Steel) bought up shares of Tennessee Coal and Iron Company. In order for this acquisition to
happen however, Morgan needed permission and most importantly assurance that an antitrust
action would not happen.

[PAGE 165]
TLDR:
America–big recession. JP Morgan takes action. How? Morgan buys Tennessee Coal and Iron
Company. With money from acquisition Morgan sets up banks. Morgan needs assurance that
the government wouldn’t kill him with antitrust laws. Roosevelt makes sure Morgan doesn’t get
killed with antitrust laws.

William Howard Taft 577 The president starting in 1909, Taft was Roosevelt’s most trusted
lieutenant and handpicked successor. Progressive reformers believed him to be one of them,
but Taft was also a restrained and moderate man, who had a meticulous regard for legal
processes.
He had great expectations placed on him, and completely failed to meet them. He came out of
the office as the most defeated president of his century, and left his party divided and the
government in the hands of a Democratic administration for the first time in 20 years.
Payne-Aldrich Tariff 577 President Taft called Congress to argue for the lowering of tariffs, which
would appease progressives in the nation. However, Taft basically chickened out when
Congress disagreed with the idea and as such a shitty Payne Aldrich Tariff was implemented
which would only reduce tariffs by a measly amount. Understandably, people got mad by how
passive this guy was playing.
Ballinger-Pinchot Dispute 577 Who is Ballinger? Richard Ballinger is a conservative lawyer who
replaced James Garfield, a conservationist who was put in the position of Secretary of the
Interior by Teddy Roosevelt. The replacement was made by President Taft.
- Ballinger tried to sell Alaskan land to a private entity, which got progressives
understandably angry.
- Louis Glavis and Gifford Pinchot brought the case to Congress
- Taft and congress backed Ballinger in the case
- Louis Glavis and Gifford Pinchot got fired
- Ballinger got out of the case unharmed somehow
- Taft is now the most hated US president
By the way Ballinger tried (unsuccessfully) to remove the protection of 1 million acres of land for
private enterprise before doing this.
“New Nationalism” 578 A new set of principles coined by Theodore Roosevelt. It made clear that
he moved away from the cautious conservatism of the first years of his presidency. He argued
that social justice was possible only through the vigorous efforts of a strong federal government.
He supported graduated income and inheritance taxes, workers’ compensation for industrial
accidents, regulation of the labor of women and children, tariff revision, and firmer regulation of
corporations.
The Progressive Party 579 Since the utter failure of Taft’s presidency left many progressives
longing for an actually good president, Teddy Roosevelt came back, but unfortunately the

[PAGE 166]
election process still turned out to be pretty hard due to Taft’s Republican support. Roosevelt
then called his supporters to Chicago for a convention, launching the Progressive Party and
having him be the presidential candidate. Unfortunately for Roosevelt however, the Democratic
party’s candidate Woodrow Wilson was too popular for him to beat.
Wilson’s “New Freedom” 579 Woodrow Wilson rose to political prominence in an unorthodox
way. He was a professor of political science at Princeton until 1902, when he was named
president of the university. He was then elected governor of New Jersey in 1910, where he
demonstrated a commitment to reform. As a presidential candidate in 1912, Wilson presented a
new progressive program called the “New Freedom.” (It was basically the opposite of
Roosevelt’s New Nationalism when it came to government size)
Lowering The Tariff 579 Wilson managed to ease both Democratic and Progressives, lowering
the protective tariff. However to make up for the loss of revenue from the tariff, congress
imposed an income tax of 1 percent for Americans earning more than 4k annually, increasing
depending on your income.
Federal Reserve Act 579 The Federal Reserve Act was passed by Congress in 1913, and
would institute 12 regional banks, and would issue a paper currency. Most importantly, Federal
Reserve notes were backed by the government and therefore served as a stable currency. This
system was highly successful, and solved a big problem with banking in the US that Taft and
Roosevelt had tried to solve years prior.
Child-Labor Laws 581 Wilson supported the Keating-Owen Act, a federal law which would
regulate child labor, prohibiting children from crossing state lines. This Act was not supported by
the Court. Another attempt was a law which would put a big tax on child labor, which too was
dismissed by the Court.

Racial and Economic Basis of Roosevelt’s Diplomacy 584 Roosevelt was a fan of the differing
“civilized and uncivilized” nations. (basically anybody that was white and not Slavic was
“civilized” while anybody that wasn’t was “uncivilized”) Therefore, Roosevelt’s foreign policy was
that he would go and intervene in the “backwards” affairs in “uncivilized” nations, and leave the
“civilized” nations alone. Just sounds like modern American foreign policy to me.
“Great White Fleet” 585 While Roosevelt won the Nobel Peace Prize for his help in ending the
Russo-Japanese War, relations between the US and Japan steadily deteriorated. Japan was not
the preeminent naval power in the Pacific and soon began to exclude American trade from
many of the territories it controlled.
To show Japan the power of the US, Roosevelt sent 16 battleships of the new American navy
(the Great WHite Fleet) on a journey around the world that included a call on Japan.
“Roosevelt Corollary” 585 Why does the textbook decide to wheel back to Roosevelt? Who
knows. You remember the Monroe Doctrine? If you do, look at it again. When Venezuela started

[PAGE 167]
to stop paying debt to Germany, Germany blockaded its ports. Roosevelt then immediately
threatened Germany, forcing their navy to piss off. This was because Roosevelt believed that
the American continent was to be controlled by the US, and having Germany show prowess in
the region was therefore ruining said control, going back to the Monroe Doctrine, Roosevelt
basically made a new version of it, this time in the name “Roosevelt Corollary”. Roosevelt used

Platt Amendment 586 In 1902, the US granted political independence to Cuba, but only after the
new government agreed to the Platt Amendment to its constitution.
The Platt Amendment barred Cuba from making treaties with other nations(Basically gave US
control of its foreign policies); it gave the US the right to intervene in Cuba to “preserve
independence, life, and property”; and it also required Cuba to permit American naval stations
on its territory.
Panamanian Revolt 587 Roosevelt was pissed that the Colombian senate refused to ratify an
agreement that gave the US perpetual rights to a canal zone across Colombia. (Keep in mind
that the Americans pressured the previous Colombian representative to agree to this). So
Roosevelt began to look for ways to bypass the Colombian government, and he found an ally in
the chief engineer of the French canal project, Philippe Bunau-Varilla.
In November 1903, a revolution began. Unlike the others that preceded this revolution, the US
backed this one, and American soldiers prevented Colombian forces from suppressing the
rebellion and, three days later, Roosevelt recognized Panama as an independent nation.
Intervention In Nicaragua 587 Quick note: Philander C Knox was a dude that worked to “Extend

it’s just trying to put something else into nicer words but you take that however you wanna).
During Taft’s presidency, a revolution broke out in Nicaragua, and the US sided with the rebels,
who received American military aid. Knox then encouraged US banks to offer loans to the new
Nicaraguan government (this basically means that with the loans, the US could be in control of
Nicaragua banks). Nicaraguans then rebelled against, this time against the pro-American
government, and Taft then landed troops in the country again, this time to support the
government and to put down the people. Classic American foreign policy.
Wilson’s Moral Diplomacy 588 During Wilson’s presidency, Mexico was going through…a time

Intervention in Mexico 589 With the permission of the Carranza government, Wilson ordered

army, in which 40 Mexicans and 12 Americans died. The US and Mexico were once again on
the brink of war, but at the last minute Wilson, who was now focused on the international crisis
in Europe, withdrew from Mexico and granted formal recognition to the Carranza regime.
Competing Alliances 589 With that fiasco out of the way, we look across the Atlantic, where

Economic Ties To Britain 589 The US sides with Britain, as we all know. This is due to the

Lusitania 590 Germans torpedoed the boat, the US got mad, and according to some historians,

Zimmermann Telegram 591 Intercepted telegram from the Germans to Mexico, in which the
Germans offer Mexico a chunk of the US if they side with Germany. The British intercepted this,
and the publication of it brought about great outrage in the US.

Stalemate 591 Neither side was gaining any ground in the war, and the Allies looked to the US
for help. Wilson was happy to oblige, and called on the US to wage war “without stint or limit”.
Russian Revolution 592 The Bolshevik Revolution happened in 1917, and Vlad Lenin
negotiated peace with the Central powers, withdrawing troops from the Western Front. The
Entente Powers had just lost a powerful ally.
Selective Service Act 592 SLIGHT ISSUE FOR THE US JOINING THE WAR: Their army was
tiny. Barely 200k total active duty soldiers split into the Army and National Guard, and neither
group was in shape to fight. The Selective Service Act was one of the first National Drafts in the
nation’s history, bringing 3 million men into the army and 2 million voluntarily, forming the
American Expeditionary Force. One cool thing was that Women were also allowed into the Army
for the first time in US history, but not allowed to participate in combat.
Another funny note: the first ever IQ test was used to measure the Intelligence Quotient of the
soldiers, but ended up putting basically all African Americans and half of the White soldiers in
the “moron” category due to a lack of education.
African American Soldiers 593 Treated horribly by Americans, even though many received high
medals of honor in other countries. These soldiers served in segregated units under white
commanders.
General John Pershing 593 The engagement of these forces in combat was intense but brief.
Not until the spring of 1918 were significant numbers of American ground troops available for
battle. Eight months later, the war was over. Under the command of General John J. Pershing,
who led the unsuccessful pursuit of Pancho Villa, the American Expeditionary Force joined the
existing Allied Forces.
Château-Thierry 594 During the war, American troops did not have to go through nearly as
1much of the trench hell that their French and British allies had to go through, and the amount of
bloodshed either side went through to gain just an inch of ground was to the point that by the
time WW1 was over, Germany had lost 15% of their army, and France 18% of their army. Due to
help from American forces, the French were able to take Chateau-Thierry in a matter of 6
weeks. It was with American intervention that the Entente had managed to gain ground so
quickly from 1918 onwards. Why the US couldn’t have done it sooner to lower the amount of
bloodshed is…not a mystery. The US wanted to sell weapons and make money, let's be honest.
Meuse-Argonne Offensive 594 On September 26, the American fighting force joined a large
assault against the Germans in the Argonne Forest that lasted nearly seven weeks. By the end
of October, despite terrible weather, they had helped push the Germans back toward their own
border and had cut the enemy’s major supply lines to the front.
High Casualty Rates 595 Here’s a tally of death on both sides of the war: The French lost 1.7
million. The Germans 2 million. Austria Hungary, 1.5 million. Italy lost 460000, Russia 1.7

[PAGE 170]
million. Turkish death toll 325000. Britain lost a third of their young to mid 20-year old
population. Despite joining late, the US lost 110000 lives to the war. This doesn’t even account
for the number of people that came home with irreversible physical harm that would leave them
unable to work for the rest of their lives. It is in this war that the term “shell-shock” is coined. Due
to the constant explosion and artillery in the war, along with the horrors of watching your best
friend’s face get torn off, many soldiers came home in irreparable mental states of shock.
Financing The War 596 By the time the war ended, the US had spent 32 billion dollars for
expenses directly related to the conflict. This was very surprising for the time, the entire federal
budget rarely exceeded 1 billion dollars before 1915, and in 1910 the nation’s entire GDP had
been only 35 billion dollars.
The US relied on two devices to finance the war, first, it launched a major drive to solicit loans
from the American people by selling “Liberty(Mutual™) Bonds” to the public. By 1920, the sale
of bonds produced 23 billion dollars. The other way was taxes–which brought in an additional
sum of nearly 10 billion dollars. Some of this was from the “excess profits” of corporations, but
much more from new, steeply increased income and inheritance taxes that ultimately rose as
high as 70 percent in some brackets.
War Industries Board 596 Financing the war was equally as hard as fighting it, and the War
Industries Board was created in 1917 to figure out where money for the war would go. Bernard
Baruch was put in control of the place by Wilson, and he basically had full control of the whole
thing, sorting out the US’s finances for the war. This wasn’t all sunshine and rainbows however,
since the agency was plagued by mismanagement and often served to better the interests of
businesses, not the military or the war effort.
Lessons Of The Managed Economy 596 The effort to organize the economy for war produced
amazing results. Hoover’s efficient organization of domestic food supplies, William McAdoo’s
success in untangling the railroads, and many more. However, in other areas, progress was so
slow that the war was over before many of the supplies ordered for it were ready. Even so,
many leaders of both government and industry emerged from the experience convinced of the
advantages of a close, cooperative relationship between the public and private sectors. Some
hoped to continue the wartime experiments in peacetime.
Ludlow Massacre 597 Despite making jobs for the economy, you gotta remember these are
American working jobs, and they aren’t fun to do whatsoever. In Ludlow Colorado, 1914, a
group of workers walked out of the mines on strike with their families, eventually moving to tents
that they set up due to being evicted. The militia was called in to initially protect the workers, but
as you can guess from the title…they ended up siding with the employers.
The militia attacked the workers, killing 39 including 11 children.

[PAGE 171]
“Great Migration” 598 One of the most important social changes of the war years, the “Great
Migration” was the movement of hundreds of thousands of African Americans from the rural
South into northern industrial cities. Like most migrations, there were both push and pull factors.
The push was the poverty, indebtedness, racism, and violence many black men and women
experienced in the South. The pull was the prospect of factory jobs in the urban North and the
opportunity to live in communities where blacks enjoyed more freedom and autonomy.
Race Riots 598 Due to the Great Migration, Whites got increasingly angry at the fact that Black
communities were growing and were beginning to move into predominantly White
neighborhoods. This incited violence. In 1917, a white mob burned down houses, shot
residents, with a death toll of 40 or more African Americans dying in the massacre.

CPI 602 The Committee on Public Information(CPI), was a government effort to rally public
support via propaganda. It was directed by Denver journalist George Creel, who spoke openly
of the importance of achieving social unity. The CPI supervised the distribution of tons of
pro-war literature and plastered the walls of offices, shops, theaters, schools, churches, and
homes.

[PAGE 172]
Espionage Act 602 ooo espionage!! This basically said that if someone is suspected of spying
on war efforts, they would receive harsh punishments like jail time, or even death. There was
also a ban on “seditious” material from the mails, which are statements that might “impugn the
motives of the government and thus encourage insubordination,” or anything that suggested
“that the government is controlled by Wall Street or munitions manufacturers, or any other
special interests.” More specifically, “espionage” referred to just about anything that was not in
support of the US in WW1.
Sedition Act 602 An extension of the Espionage Act. It expanded the meaning of the Espionage
act to make any public expression of opposition to the war illegal; in practice, it allowed officials
to prosecute anyone who criticized the president or the government.
Repressing Dissent 602 State and local governments, corporations, universities, and private
citizens contributed to the climate of repression as well. Mobs sprang up to “discipline” those
who dared challenge the war. A dissident Protestant clergyman in Cincinnati was pulled from his
bed one night, dragged to a nearby hill, and whipped “in the name of the women and children of
Belgium” by a mob.
“100 Percent Americanism” 602 Groups of citizens who considered themselves “respectable”
tasked themselves with the responsibility of weeding out the “disloyal”. These vigilante terrorist
groups were groups such as the American Protective League and the National Security League,
who would often bring violence to people they considered “disloyal” such as Irish, who were
picked on because historically Irish people had been in opposition to the British, and Jews since
there were anti-semetic views and laws in Russia. Most of all, German Americans were heavily
targeted, despite many German Americans being possibly the most loyal to the American flag,
renaming their foods to stuff like “liberty cabbage” or “liberty sausage” when the war started.
This started a new wave of anti-immigration and Xenophobia in the US.
Wilson’s Idealistic Vision 603 On January 8th, 1918, Woodrow Wilson presented his 14 points
on why he is fighting WW1. The 14 points were not only a set of points that would justify
Wilson’s decision to stay in the war, but most importantly present a peace plan for the
near-to-end war and how Europe would recover and borders would change in Europe. The
important thing to note here is that by presenting the 14 points, the US is therefore declaring
control over the rebuilding of Europe after the war, similar to how the US would declare control
over Japan’s reconstruction after WWII.
Lenin’s Challenge 603 Another big reason behind Wilson’s 14 Points was made was because
Lenin just 3 weeks prior had made a similar speech, promising to help Europe and bring major
reform and similar peace as soon as the war was over. Wilson saw this as directly rivaling US
control of reform in Europe, and as such Wilson made it clear that Lenin would not be allowed
full control over the reform of Europe, and that the US would try their best to take that control.
Allied Intransigence 603 Quick Note: Intransigence basically means stubbornness. As the war
was looking more and more like a win for the Entente, negotiations for peace were already

[PAGE 173]
being made. Or rather, arrangements for peace. This is because the US was basically in full
control of peace deals, largely due to the US putting themselves on a moral pedestal but also
because after Britain had lost a third of their young men, and the French had lost similar
numbers, the 2 countries were very eager to see a full decimation of Germany, and a capture
and execution of the Kaiser.
The Big Four 604 As much as the French and British government were eager to beat down the
Germans who had, by the way, lost similar numbers as the French and British, the general
populace was very eager to see peace. When US President Woodrow Wilson arrived in Paris,
records say the largest crowd in French history was there to greet him. The Big Four refers to
the 4 figures who met in Paris in December of 1918: David Lloyd George representing Britain,
George Clemenceau representing France, and Vittorio Orlando representing Italy. Besides
discussing the general peace of Europe for the post war era, there were also talks about the
Bolsheviks. Soon later, the US landed troops to assist some 60k Czech troops to escape the
Soviet Union, and despite wanting to stay out of conflict, it became increasingly more difficult for
the US and the new Soviet Union to see eye to eye. Woodrow Wilson would not recognize the
Bolshevik as the Russian government, and from here on out until WWII, there was no world
where the US and Soviet Union would dare see peace between one another.
Wilson’s Retreat 604 Wilson was not able to capture the full support of all the European nations
in the Paris Peace meeting, and unable to get all of his wanted reforms. For one, many of the
European nations were unwilling to get rid of their colonies, and if they were, another country
would eagerly take up the former colony. Such is the case with Germany, which gave up their
Asian colonies, but Japan immediately took those colonies for their own.
Reparations 604 The next topic in the Paris Peace meeting were the reparations to be paid.
Initially, it was agreed that Germany would pay a staggering 56 billion dollars, which was later
lowered to 9 billion. Even with such a drastic cut from the initial amount, the battered Germany
couldn’t pay it off.
League Of Nations 605 The most important part of the Paris Peace meeting was the creation of
the League Of Nations. This org would attempt to keep peace between the countries in the
league, oversee International Affairs, and prevent wars.
Wilson’s Intransigence 605 Note: Intransigence again, means stubbornness. Now one thing we
haven’t yet considered is the views of Americans back at home and how they would view the
Paris Peace treaty. Americans were not too happy, since America had been in a period of
European isolation before and the sudden change of pace did not sit with them very well. Wilson
attempted to convince the Senate that his actions were justified but was met with harsh
criticism. Wilson then presented the Versailles Treaty (named after the palace where the
negotiations had taken place). Wilson would not take no for an answer and also refused any
sort of compromise about the treaty. Records say that it was due to Wilson’s deteriorating health
that he was so hell-bent on keeping the Versailles Treaty exactly as it was.

[PAGE 174]
Henry Cabot Lodge 605 The Senate and Wilson were now at a standoff. Neither side was willing
to budge, Wilson would not take any criticism about the Versailles Treaty, and the Senate would
not take the treaty as it was without some sort of compromise. Henry Cabot Lodge was the one
that would oppose Wilson the most, citing how he straight up hated Wilson.
League Membership Rejected 606 Wilson refused to consider any amendments to the
proposals to join the League, and the treaty died out as nobody could really vote it into
existence, and people stopped caring.
New Social Environment 606 People were not really caring about the Peace Conference, as
there were a couple of problems back at home to deal with. The Economy went hahaha boom
and had a huge postwar recession, and most of American became more conservative to cope
with it.
Postwar Recession 606 Yeah, during the 1910s the stock market was doing well, but then the
economic bubble burst, and the entire market went kaboom. Millions of people lost their jobs,
and many went bankrupt. Making things even worse, the factories that were still standing used
this opportunity to regress and start paying less for workers and stuff like that
Boston Police Strike 607 There were a lot of strikes in 1919, and one of the more notable ones

Garvey also founded the United Negro Improvement Association (UNIA), which launched a

Philip Randolph 620 The AFL(American Federation of Labor) often would try and discriminate

Protecting the Open Shop 621 The open shop is a system where no worker is required to join a
union. Protection of the open shop was needed because corporate leaders spread the idea that
unionism(protection of workers rights) was harmful to corporations.
Protection of the open shop created the “American Plan” which was soon endorsed by the
National Association of Manufactures in 1920. This campaign busted many unions across the
US.
Mechanized Farming 622 What’s better than farming? Farming with Machines! Tractors,

farmland. In addition the creation of pesticides and fertilizers would lead to even more food
being produced.
● However, the population was not growing as fast as the Agriculture Industry, leading to a
massive decrease in Food prices as the supply greatly outmatched the demand.
“Parity” 622 Because of the food prices being stupidly and unsustainably low, many farmers
supported the idea of Parity. Parity is pretty much the idea that the government would set the
price of food at a price where farmers would always recoup their funds no matter what the
markets might look like.

were made that suggested that the war was pointless, and that people were justified in not
